# The Second Son of the House of Bells

# Chapter One: The Morning Pitch

Cass was awake before the signal bell.

He lay on his cot in the dark and listened to his father moving through the workshop below — the scrape of the forge rake, the clink of a pitch-gauge set down on stone. The sounds came up through the floorboards without effort. This close to the Bell Tower, everything carried.

He pulled on his trousers and went down.

The workshop was warm. His father had been there for an hour at least, maybe two — the forge coals were already settled from orange to red, and the air held the density of a space that had been breathed in for a while. Linseed oil and cold bronze, and underneath it the char of yesterday's casting that never quite left the stonework.

Eddan Bellwright stood at the lathe, turning a clapper. He did not look up.

Cass crossed to the tool bench and began laying out the pitch-gauges in order of diameter, smallest to largest. He knew what his father would need. They had done this together since Cass was eight years old, and the routine had its own grammar, as clear as a well-composed interval: this before that, here before there.

"Seventh of Ash Street?" Cass said.

"Mm."

"Still a quarter-flat?"

"More like three-sixteenths." His father tilted the clapper on the lathe, eyeing the curve. "Fish market's been complaining since autumn."

"I heard it in autumn. I told you in autumn."

"You tell me a lot of things."

Cass picked up the nearest gauge and checked it for burrs. "I'm just saying I was right."

"You're often right." Eddan's voice had the quality it got in the workshop — flattened, focused, most of his attention on the metal. "It's one of your more irritating qualities."

Cass set the gauge down. He wasn't sure if that was a compliment. With his father, the two were close enough together that it was usually easier not to investigate.

The bells in progress hung along the iron frames that lined the eastern wall — seven of them at various stages, each one a different district's voice. Not truly silent. If Cass pressed his palm flat against the largest, the Third of the Weavers' Quarter, he could feel a faint tremor in the metal that never quite settled. The bells hummed to themselves, the accumulated vibration of two hundred years of being struck.

"Gauge," his father said.

Cass handed him the third one along. Their fingers met on the brass handle, and Eddan held it against the half-formed clapper, reading the measurement by touch and lamplight.

"Close," Eddan said. He set the gauge down and went back to the lathe.

Cass pulled a stool to the bench and began cleaning the smaller gauges with a strip of oiled cloth. Outside, the bakeries on the south side of Civic Square were already working — the rhythmic knock of dough against stone, the creak of oven doors. Sound traveled far at this hour.

He had stepped wide around the door on the left when he came through the kitchen, without deciding to, like favoring a bad knee.

"The Third's tremor is worse," Cass said, because the silence had shifted into the kind that felt like it wanted filling. "The Weavers' Quarter one. You can feel it if you put your hand on the frame."

His father didn't answer immediately. The lathe turned.

"I know," Eddan said finally.

"Are we going to do something about it?"

"After the Seventh."

"The Seventh's been after for two months."

The lathe stopped. Eddan set the clapper down on the stone bench with a sound like a period at the end of a sentence, and looked at Cass for the first time since he'd come downstairs. His face in the lamplight was unreadable in the way it had been for years — not closed, exactly, just worn smooth, like a step where the detail had been walked off.

"After the Seventh," he said again. "Hand me the fine cloth."

Cass handed him the fine cloth.

That was the whole argument. Here in the workshop, in the hour before the Civic Pitch, the silence between them had a different quality than the silence at the dinner table. The dinner table silence was a held breath. This one was a rest in a measure — intentional, structural, part of the composition. He had learned not to push past it. Pushing past it never got him anywhere, and it made his father's hands shake, and then Cass felt guilty about the shaking, and the whole thing was exhausting.

Forty-five minutes after sunrise, his father put down his tools.

They went out into the Square together, Eddan with his strike-hammer and the small tuning bell he carried for pitch reference, Cass a half-step behind. The Square was already filling. Not crowded — the Civic Pitch drew witnesses, not performers, and most people kept to the edges, the south arcade and the bakery steps, standing with their morning bread or their hands in their pockets, waiting.

The Bell Tower rose at the Square's center, limestone gone gray-white with age, the bells invisible from below except as dark shapes in the open arches. Cass had climbed up there twice — once with his father, once with Perin, who had held the edge of the arch with one hand and leaned out over the city and laughed at something. He couldn't remember what. The memory had that quality: the laugh clear, the content gone.

His father struck the signal note on the tuning bell. A single clean A. The city's first word of the day.

Then the Tower answered.

The Civic Pitch began with the Anchor, that low foundational tone that settled in Cass's back teeth, and built from there in the sequence Aldric Bellwright had composed two hundred years ago and no one had altered since. Seven bells in the first phrase, a rising progression that resolved on the fifth degree. Twelve bells in the second, more complex, a series of overlapping intervals that created standing harmonics in the Square's stone walls. He felt it in his back teeth first, then his jaw, then the bones behind his ears.

He stood with his hands at his sides and let it happen.

The Pitch was not beautiful. That was what he always had to remind himself, watching the faces of people who had traveled in from outside the city — standing in the Square with their mouths slightly open, expressions soft with something that looked like awe. It wasn't beautiful. It was correct. The difference between a painting of a doorway and an actual door. The one you could walk through.

He tracked the intervals automatically. Third, fifth, octave, descending fourth. Resolution to the root. The sequence was patterned into him like the workshop's smell, and he let it move through his chest and ribs and the small bones behind his ears without fighting it.

There. In the third phrase.

He had been hearing it for six months. A low resonance, older than the bells themselves seemed to be, a pressure in his sternum that was not pain and was not pleasure but something that occupied the space where those two things met. It sat beneath the Pitch the way a current sits beneath still water — present to anything that went deep enough. A question. That was the closest he could come to describing it, and he had never described it to anyone, so the description had stayed rough and unrefined in his own thinking. A question shaped like a sound.

He pressed his tongue to the roof of his mouth. Held it there.

The Pitch moved through its final phrase — all forty-seven bells, the full sequence, the resolution chord that landed like a hand flat on a table. Done. Certain.

For thirty seconds, nothing moved. The birds went silent — the sparrows and the starlings that nested in the Tower's lower arches simply stopped. Not startled-stopped. They stopped the way a held note stops: complete, intentional, as though they were waiting for something. He had counted it once. Thirty seconds, give or take. Then they started again, and the Square filled with the ordinary sounds of morning.

He had not mentioned it to anyone.

The pressure in his chest faded slowly. He breathed out.

His father was already walking back toward the workshop, the tuning bell in his left hand. From behind, you couldn't see the tremor in his hands. From behind, he was just a man walking across a square in the early morning, carrying his tools home.

---

The Academy was a twenty-minute walk from the Bell Quarter, east along the Square's arcade and then up the slope through the merchant streets where the song-brokers set up their morning stations. Cass took the longer route past the grain market most days. He told himself it was because the light was better on the east side of the slope. This was partly true.

He heard the under-note before he saw the stall.

A faint pressure behind his left eye, there and then almost gone — a small thing, social-grade, the kind of minor evasion that happened a hundred times a day in any city where words had weight. He slowed without thinking and scanned the market.

Two merchants at a grain stall, a song-broker between them, composing a delivery contract in real time. The broker was good — her phrasing was clean, her intervals precisely placed, the three-clause structure clear enough that Cass could follow the architecture from fifteen feet away. First clause: quantity and grade. Second clause: payment terms. Third clause: delivery dates.

The second merchant — a short man with a gray coat and a counting board under his arm — sang the ratification note a half-beat late. Not late enough to void anything. Late enough that Cass's ear caught it.

The under-note was already fading by the time he identified the source. The man wasn't lying about the money or the grain. He was lying about the dates — the soft evasion of someone who had not yet arranged his carts and was hoping the problem would resolve itself before the contract came due. Small stakes. The needle behind Cass's eye was already gone.

The sharp kind. A clean signal — someone saying something they knew wasn't true. It was the easiest kind to read. The other kinds were harder.

He adjusted his route by three stalls' width and walked past quickly. The flux merchant on the corner had set up a new display of solder bars, and a small handwritten sign that read CERTIFIED PITCH-CLEAN, which was either true or the most optimistic claim on the street.

"How certified?" Cass said, not quite stopping.

The merchant — a stout woman with blue-stained fingers and the slightly aggressive cheerfulness of someone who had been doing this since before dawn — looked up. "Tested against the morning Pitch. Every bar."

"Which bell?"

She blinked. "What?"

"Which bell did you test them against. The Anchor? The Seventh? They're not all the same reference."

A pause. She had not expected this question. Most people didn't ask this question. "The — the Anchor. The big one."

"Right," Cass said. "That's fine, then." He kept walking. The bars were probably fine. The Anchor was the most stable bell in the sequence. But the sign still bothered him — *certified* implied a process, and a process implied someone had thought it through, and he was not convinced anyone had.

The tongue-to-roof habit was a reflex now. He knew it didn't help. Perin had taught it to him when Cass was nine, the summer before the contract, sitting in the workshop with their legs hanging off the edge of the casting table. *Press your tongue up*, Perin had said. *Like you're trying to touch your own skull from the inside. It doesn't do anything, but it gives your body something to do besides panic.* He'd said it like he said most things: matter-of-fact, a little dry, as though the advice being useless was part of the point. You stayed in your body. You didn't float out of it just because something hurt.

The slope steepened toward the Academy District. The streets changed character as he climbed — broader, better paved, the buildings set back behind small arcaded walkways. He passed three other students in gray wool, walking in pairs, not speaking. Morning. The Academy did not encourage casual conversation before classes. Words had legal weight; casual use was considered wasteful. Good bronze spent on a practice pour.

Cass had his own feelings about this.

The Academy smelled of chalk and resin — sharp enough that after three months of enrollment it had embedded itself in the gray wool of his uniform. Something between pine and turpentine. He sometimes caught it on himself at the workshop and felt the two worlds overlap in a way that was mostly unpleasant.

He found his seat in the theory hall, third row from the front, left side. Laid out his notation paper and his pitch-stylus. The room filled around him — twenty students in gray, settling into chairs, talking in the careful, measured way people talked when they'd been trained to think of speech as currency.

A girl named Lenne Ashvale dropped into the seat to his right. She had a stack of notation paper so thick it was bound with a leather strap, and she set it on the desk with the confidence of someone who had done all the reading and wanted the room to know it without having to say so.

"You're in my row," Cass said.

"The row doesn't have your name on it."

"I sit there every day."

"And yet." She unknotted the leather strap and spread her papers with the particular neatness of someone who had a system. "Proctor Fen is going to demonstrate binding intervals again. I can tell."

"He does it every third session."

"He did it last session. And the one before."

Cass looked up from his pitch-stylus. "That's twice in a row."

"Yes."

"So either he's lost count of his own sessions, or he thinks we're all still getting it wrong."

"We *are* all still getting it wrong," Lenne said. "Ossian ratified in the wrong key last week and didn't notice for six measures."

"I noticed."

She gave him a sideways look. "What key was it actually in?"

"D-flat. He thought he was in C."

"How far off was the interval?"

"Far enough." He picked up his pitch-stylus and turned it over. "The resin smell is worse today. Is it always this bad, or did someone refinish a floor?"

"Practice room three," Lenne said, without looking up from her notes. "They refinished it over the weekend. It'll be like this for a week."

"A week."

"Minimum." She paused, stylus hovering. "Do you actually find it worse, or are you just complaining?"

"Both," Cass said. "Those aren't mutually exclusive."

A boy named Ossian appeared at the end of the row, looked at the available seats, and decided to sit two rows back instead, which Cass considered the first good decision he'd seen Ossian make. Ossian settled in and immediately began telling someone beside him about a contract he'd witnessed at the market. "The ratification was clean," Ossian said. "Perfect third, held for a full six seconds." He said it with the confidence of someone who had been right about something recently and wanted the room to know.

Cass's eye twitched.

"Six seconds," he said, quietly enough that only Lenne could hear.

She glanced back at Ossian, then at Cass. "Was it not?"

"Four. Maybe four and a half."

"You were there?"

"I walked past. I heard it."

Lenne considered this. She had the expression she sometimes got when she was working something out — a slight tension around the eyes, her stylus moving in small absent arcs against her palm, fingering intervals. "A four-second ratification is still valid. The minimum's four."

"I know it's valid. I'm saying he's wrong about the six."

"Why does it matter if he's wrong about the six?"

"Because he's going to go through his whole career telling clients their ratifications held for six seconds when they held for four, and the difference matters when someone challenges the contract, and he won't even know to check." Cass set the stylus down. "He's performing certainty he doesn't have."

Lenne looked at him for a moment. "You could tell him."

"I could." He picked the stylus back up. "I'm not going to."

"Why not?"

"Because then I'd have to explain how I know, and that conversation takes longer than I have this morning."

She opened her mouth, closed it, and turned back to her notes. He had the sense she was filing the question away for later. One of Lenne's more predictable qualities. She didn't let things go. She just agreed to table them.

Proctor Fen arrived without ceremony, set his leather case on the desk, and looked at the class with the expression of a man who had been disappointed by rooms full of people before and saw no reason to expect today would be different.

"We're doing binding intervals," he said.

Lenne didn't say *I told you so.* She didn't have to. Cass could hear it in the way she straightened her stack of papers.

The morning's primary exercise was a three-clause grain contract. Apparently what the Academy considered suitable for beginners. Cass drafted it in the assigned key, noticed the lame chord in the third clause that no one else seemed to hear — a binding that gripped one party without gripping the other — a handshake where only one hand squeezed, and composed a corrected version in a different key on a separate sheet.

He submitted the assigned version. The correct one he folded into his coat pocket.

Some fights weren't worth the conversation they'd start.

On the way out, Lenne fell into step beside him in the corridor, which was either coincidence or not.

"The third clause," she said.

He kept walking. "What about it."

"You wrote it in F. Fen asked for D."

"I know what Fen asked for."

"The third clause in D has a diminished seventh that doesn't resolve cleanly," she said. It wasn't a question. She was working through it out loud. A thing she did. "If you extend the contract past thirty days, the interval goes lame."

"Yes."

"So you wrote it in F because F resolves cleanly over thirty days."

"F resolves cleanly over sixty."

She was quiet for a few steps. The corridor did its Academy trick of carrying sound wrong — a conversation twenty feet ahead arrived too clearly, something about a dinner and someone's mother. "You should have submitted the F version," Lenne said.

"Then I'd have to explain it to Fen."

"Yes."

"And Fen would want to know why I heard the diminished seventh as lame when no one else did, and then we'd be having a different conversation than the one about grain contracts."

She looked at him. "What kind of conversation would we be having?"

"The kind I'm not having," Cass said, and pushed through the outer door into the gray morning air.

---

He left by the eastern gate.

The habit was recent — two weeks, maybe three. He didn't examine it too closely. The eastern gate faced away from the Bell Quarter, away from the Civic Square, which meant that when he turned back toward home he had to turn around, had to make the choice to look.

He turned around.

The Bell Tower was visible above the roofline, limestone pale against the morning sky. He could see the arches where the bells hung, dark shapes in the light. He could see the ring of old-growth trees along the bowl's rim, hornbeam and oak, their branches just beginning to show the first thin green of the season.

The harmonic was still in his chest. Fading like the Pitch's tone — the sound gone but the vibration persisting. He had been feeling it for six months. He had no name for it. He had not told his father, because his father's hands shook worst on mornings when the Pitch ran long, and the two things felt connected in a way Cass could not yet articulate.

This was not the under-note. The under-note had texture — greasy, curdled, the sound of a mouth shaping words the throat wanted to refuse. This was the opposite. Clean. Something that rang without dissonance, that had been sitting in the Pitch since before he was born. Waiting, maybe. Or just there, like the limestone, like the bowl itself.

Behind him, two students were arguing about the resolution of a diminished chord, their voices carrying in the Academy's odd acoustics — the words arriving too clearly, as though the building wanted him to hear. He let them pass.

The morning sun had cleared the bowl's rim. Long gold across the limestone arcades. He stood at the gate and let the harmonic finish fading, as he would wait for a bell's tone to finish before striking the next. You gave it space to be complete.

Then he shouldered his bag and walked home.
---

# Chapter Two: Song-Broker Weather

The Academy's theory rooms smelled of chalk dust and floor resin — sharp on the first breath, gone by the second, absorbed into the general smell of sixty students who had been in the same building since dawn.

Proctor Fen was demonstrating binding intervals again. He did this every third session, regardless of where the syllabus said they were meant to be, because he believed — and said so, at least once a month — that a student who couldn't ratify a basic contract under pressure had learned nothing, regardless of their written assessments.

"Volunteer," he said, with the expression of a man who had been disappointed by volunteers before.

No one moved. Cass kept his eyes on the notation board, where Fen had chalked out a descending fifth with the slightly aggressive strokes of someone who had written the same interval eight thousand times and still found people getting it wrong.

"Volunteer," Fen said again. "Or I assign one."

A boy named Ossian stood up, faster than Cass expected from Ossian, who usually waited to see which way the room was leaning before he committed to anything.

"Thank you, Ossian." Fen produced his pitch-pipe — small brass, worn smooth on the barrel where his thumb rested. He sounded the reference note, a clean A, and waited for it to settle. The theory room was in the old building, tight-corridored stone, and the A filled it differently than it would have in the limestone arcades: more absorption, less carry. Cass let it settle in his chest anyway.

"Ratification first," Fen said. "Major third from the reference. You take the upper voice. I'll take the lower."

The major third: two notes that locked together like a handshake. When you heard it done right, there was a small structural click to it — not loud, not dramatic, just the acoustic fact of two pitches finding each other and agreeing.

Ossian sang the upper note. Fen sang the lower. The interval locked, clean and correct. Cass heard the click.

"Now the binding," Fen said. "Descending fifth from the ratification note."

A descending fifth was the door closing. The upper note fell to the lower, and the distance between them — seven half-steps, a specific ratio the air between two people could hold — sealed whatever the ratification had opened. Up was refusal. Down was done.

They sang it.

And then something went wrong.

It happened at the bottom of the interval, in Ossian's voice. The note arrived — but it arrived somewhere it wasn't supposed to be. Not dramatically wrong. Not wrong enough that a person without training would catch it. Just slightly, specifically, precisely flat.

Cass's jaw tightened. A small involuntary thing, like touching a bruise.

He knew that sensation. Not the under-note — nothing like the under-note — but the body's recognition of a note that had missed its target. The interval had collapsed into something that was not a binding and not quite a rejection but a third thing: a provisional agreement. Legal force until sundown. No longer.

Fen stopped singing. He looked at Ossian with unsurprised disappointment.

"Well," he said.

"I'm sorry," Ossian said. "The acoustics in here —"

Not the clean needle of a deliberate lie. Something muddier — the texture of a person who had repeated something to themselves until the repetition felt like truth. The under-note couldn't quite decide what to do with it. It flickered behind Cass's eye, there and not there, like a candle in a draft.

"The acoustics in here are identical to the acoustics in the Civic Court's secondary chamber, which is where you will be composing contracts for paying clients if you don't flat your ratification notes." Fen looked at the class. "What did Ossian just produce?"

"Provisional agreement," said three people at once.

"Expiring when?"

"Sundown," said two people.

"Which means," Fen said, "that Ossian is currently provisionally bound to carry my books until sundown. The question before the class is whether this binding is enforceable under Codex Volume One, Section Twelve, given that the provisional agreement was produced in a pedagogical context and not a commercial one, and given that I have not yet stated what terms the provisional agreement covers."

"The terms have to be stated before ratification," said a girl in the second row. "You can't retroactively assign terms to a provisional."

"Volume One, Section Four. Ratification requires stated terms."

"So Ossian's not bound."

"Ossian produced the interval. The interval has acoustic force regardless of whether the terms were stated."

"That's not how it —"

"It's how the physics work. The Codex doesn't repeal physics."

Cass was watching Ossian, who was standing at the front of the room calculating how much this was going to cost him. He felt a faint sympathy. The flat had been easy to make. The stone rooms had genuinely different acoustics, and a nervous student singing for the class could miss the bottom note without hearing it — the ear wanted the note to be right and sometimes convinced the brain it was.

The debate continued for several minutes. Fen watched it with the quiet satisfaction of a man who had planned this exact outcome. Eventually he raised one hand.

"The answer," he said, "is that it depends on which proctor is marking your case. Which is the most honest thing I will say to you all term. Carry my books, Ossian."

Ossian sat down.

From two rows ahead and one seat left, a voice said: "The terms were implicit in the pedagogical context. Demonstration of a binding produces an implicit offer of the demonstration's natural consequence. Ossian accepted the demonstration voluntarily. The terms were present. He's bound."

Fen turned. Cass turned with him.

The girl who had spoken was making a small notation on her paper, fingering an interval against the desktop — two fingers, quick and unconscious, working through a phrase while thinking about something else. Dark hair pulled back without ceremony. The upright posture of someone trained in breath control since before they could read.

Lenne Ashvale. He had been in the same theory class with her for two months and had exchanged perhaps thirty words with her, most of them about notation.

"Interesting interpretation," Fen said. "Cite the Codex."

"Volume Two, Section Nine. Implicit terms in demonstration contracts." She looked up from her paper. "Usually applied to commercial demonstrations. There's no case law that explicitly extends it to pedagogical ones. But there's no case law that explicitly excludes it either."

"So Ossian is bound or not bound depending on a gap in the Codex."

"Yes."

Fen smiled. Unusual. "Ossian," he said. "Carry my books."

Ossian stood up again, slowly, visibly lost in a legal argument that had left him two turns ago.

Cass put his hand up.

Fen looked at him. The class looked at him. He was aware of Lenne Ashvale looking at him.

"Volume Two, Section Nine requires the demonstration to have commercial intent," he said. "Implicit or otherwise. There's no commercial intent in a classroom exercise. The section doesn't apply."

Lenne turned around in her seat. "Intent doesn't have to be present at the time of ratification. It can be inferred from the context of the parties."

"He's a proctor demonstrating an interval. She's a student arguing he should make a student carry his books. Where's the commercial context?"

"The outcome has commercial implications. Ossian's grade reflects his performance. His grade affects his licensure prospects. His licensure is commercial."

"That's three steps removed from the ratification."

"The Codex doesn't specify how many steps."

"The Codex also doesn't say you can extend a commercial section to cover whatever you want by building a long enough chain."

Fen was watching this with the expression of a man who had planned for a different outcome and found this one acceptable.

"The section applies," Lenne said. "Ossian's bound."

"The section doesn't apply," Cass said. "Ossian's not bound. And either way, Proctor Fen can't actually make him carry the books, because a provisional agreement requires both parties to have legal standing in the transaction, and Fen is an Academy employee conducting a demonstration, which means he's acting in an institutional capacity, which means the binding runs to the Academy, not to Fen personally, and the Academy hasn't authorized book-carrying as a penalty."

The room was quiet for a moment.

"Volume Three, Section Two," Cass added. "Institutional capacity and personal liability."

Lenne turned back around to face the front. She picked up her pen. He couldn't see her face.

"Ossian," Fen said, "sit down." He put the pitch-pipe back in his coat. "Both arguments have merit. Both arguments have flaws. This is the condition you will practice in for your entire career. The Codex is not a complete document. It never has been." He looked at the notation board. "Read chapters four through six before next session. We'll be examining provisional agreements in commercial contexts. Actual commercial contexts."

---

In the corridor outside, Cass was pulling his bag onto his shoulder when Lenne fell into step beside him.

"Volume Three, Section Two," she said. "Nice."

"It's in the Codex."

"Everything's in the Codex. The question is whether Fen agreed with you."

"He didn't disagree."

"He also didn't agree. He said both arguments have merit."

"Which means neither of us lost."

"Which means neither of us won." She held the door to the arcade and he walked through without thinking about it. Morning light came in level through the limestone columns, bright and cold. "You heard the flat before Fen's expression changed."

He looked at her sideways.

"You went still," she said. "Right when Ossian hit the bottom note."

"The room's got poor absorption. The flat was audible."

"I heard flat too. I didn't hear how flat."

He considered not answering. There was no good reason not to answer. "Quarter-tone," he said.

She walked beside him for a few steps. Not looking at him — looking at the arcade columns, or at something past them. "I heard wrong. I didn't hear *how* wrong."

"Stone rooms. Less scatter."

"Is that all it is."

That wasn't a question. He didn't answer it.

"Volume Two, Section Nine really does apply," she said. "The chain of commercial implication is legitimate."

"The chain is too long. You can connect anything to commercial intent if you're allowed unlimited steps."

"You can connect anything to anything if you're allowed unlimited steps. That's not an argument against the chain, it's an argument for limiting step-count. Which the Codex should specify and doesn't."

"So your argument is that the Codex has a gap."

"My argument is that the Codex has a gap and the gap favors my interpretation until someone closes it." She adjusted the strap of her folder. "My mother keeps her voting records in a separate book — the one she doesn't show the Academy. Even she knows the gaps are there."

He stopped walking. She stopped a step later and turned back.

"That's not what an argument is," he said. "An argument is a reason the interpretation is correct. 'The Codex doesn't say I'm wrong' isn't a reason."

"It's a legal reason."

"It's a procedural reason. Those aren't the same thing."

She looked at him for a moment with the expression of someone recalibrating a measurement. Not offended. Interested in the same way she'd been interested in the demonstration — like she was taking a reading.

"Ossian was bound," she said.

"Ossian was not bound."

"We'll see what Fen's assessment says."

"We will."

She turned into the covered walkway toward the upper arcades. He went the other way, toward the gate and the market. He was aware of her watching him go for a second before she didn't.

He was also aware that he'd enjoyed that more than he'd expected to, which was an annoying thing to notice.

---

The market near the Fade Line ran along the lower edge of the Academy District and down two streets into the territory where the acoustic rules went soft. You could tell where the boundary was on a calm day by the volume: inner-district conversations carried in that muffled, careful way of a city that knew its words traveled, and then at a certain point the conversations got louder and less careful, and people argued at normal human volume, and a man three stalls down could shout a price without the whole street hearing it.

Today there was a headwind coming down from the bowl's rim, pushing the Fade Line inward by half a block. The market noise pressed at him from all sides — stall-keepers calling prices, the clatter of copper coins struck against testing surfaces, bread and tallow cut through with dye-stink from a cloth merchant's cart that hadn't yet sold enough to justify the trip home.

He needed flux. Grade-three tin flux for bell-metal repairs, the kind his father used for maintenance work that didn't require the forge at full heat — tightening a hairline crack, re-seating a clapper pin. The merchant who stocked it was on the third row, past the cloth and the grain and past the stall with the woman who had been tuning her bronze weights slightly sharp for six months.

Cass had noticed the weights three months ago. A sharp weight didn't look wrong; it looked like every other weight on the stall. But when you struck it against the testing block, it rang half a tone above where it should have been, and a contract ratified using it as a reference tone would be sitting on a crooked foundation. The woman's customers were all from the outer districts, where the Fade Line made acoustic boundaries ambiguous already. He had told himself this was not his business. He was mostly convinced.

The flux merchant was a short man named Brae who kept his stock in labeled jars and knew every grade by sight without checking the labels. Cass told him what he needed, Brae found it, they agreed on a price in three exchanges — no song-broker required, both of them speaking in the clipped shorthand of a transaction that didn't need legal force to be real. Cass paid in two copper quarters, struck them against the testing block out of habit, heard the clean A of both. He pocketed the flux.

He was turning to leave when the argument started two stalls down.

A cloth merchant and a woman in a weaver's apron, both past negotiating and not yet at resolution. The cloth merchant had the specific expression of someone who believed he was right and was finding that being right was not, by itself, sufficient. The woman was pointing at a length of cloth with the focused energy of someone making an argument she had already made twice.

Between them, a song-broker was working.

Cass stopped.

He had the flux. He had an assignment due tomorrow. He was already late. He watched anyway.

The broker was composing on the fly. What market brokers did — no resonance chamber, no hour of preparation, just two angry people and the ambient acoustics of a market day and whatever was in your head. She was humming to herself, working through the interval structure before she sang it aloud, moving through the key of F, building toward the ratification sequence.

Cass heard it forming.

The major third was correct — that handshake-click of two notes locking together. The ratification note was correct. The descending fifth began correctly, fell the right distance, and then —

Something clenched in his jaw. Back teeth, on the left side, like biting down on something that shouldn't be there.

He stood very still.

The fifth had almost landed. Almost. The resolution tone — the final note that said *done, settled, no going back* — was sitting a fraction sharp of center. Not enough to hear in the ordinary sense. Not enough that the cloth merchant would feel anything. Not enough that the woman in the apron would feel anything, or that the broker herself would catch it, composing in real time in a headwind with the Fade Line pressing inward and the note almost right.

But his jaw had caught it before his mind named it.

He knew what this was. He had read about it in Volume One, Section Fourteen: a lame chord. An interval that looked complete and wasn't. A binding that would hold one party without holding the other — which one depended on which voice sang which part of the ratification, and he couldn't determine that yet from here, but the structure was wrong, and in two minutes the broker would sing it into the air between these two people and one of them would walk away believing they had a binding agreement.

He had taken one step toward the stall before he stopped himself.

He didn't have credentials. He didn't have guild standing. He couldn't walk up to a licensed broker in the middle of a commission and say *your resolution tone is sharp* without being able to cite a volume and section, without being able to put his name to a formal objection, without being a person the market recognized as someone whose ear meant something legally.

Three years left at the Academy. Then a license examination. Then a probationary period. Then, if everything went right, he would be a person whose observations could be recorded and acted on.

He stood at the edge of the stall and watched the broker finish composing. Watched her pitch the ratification interval. Watched both parties sing their parts, heard the lame chord settle into the air between them with the faint structural wrongness of a bell that had been repaired instead of recast.

The cloth merchant shook the woman's hand. Both of them looked satisfied.

One of them was wrong about what had just happened. He didn't know which one yet.

He turned away and walked toward the market's edge.

---

There was an old man standing where the stalls thinned out and the road started down toward the Weavers' District. Large, going soft through the middle, with hands stained a permanent blue-green that Cass recognized as dye-work — the color you got from decades near indigo vats, the kind that didn't wash out. He had one hand cupped around his left ear, the posture of someone with partial deafness trying to hear something specific in a loud space.

He was watching the cloth merchant's stall.

He said, to no one in particular, at the comfortable volume of a man from the outer districts where talking to yourself in public was not considered remarkable: "Forty years of that. Academy trains them to compose fast, not right. The people who get hurt by a lame chord aren't the ones who can afford to appeal it."

He wasn't talking to Cass. He was talking to the market, or to himself, or to the general principle of the thing. He watched the cloth merchant stack his recovered bolt of fabric. Nothing in his face suggested surprise.

Cass kept walking.

He made it six steps before he stopped. He turned back.

"You heard it," he said.

The old man looked at him. He had the eyes of someone who had been underestimated long enough to find it useful.

"Heard what," the old man said.

"The lame chord. You were watching the stall before the ratification. You knew it was coming."

"I've been watching that broker work this market for three years." He lowered his hand from his ear. "Don't need to hear it sharp to know what she builds."

"She's been doing it for three years?"

"Not always. Not on purpose, maybe. But the outer-district clients don't come back to appeal, so she never finds out when she's wrong." He looked at Cass with a directness that had nothing polite in it. "You heard it."

"The resolution tone was sharp."

"How sharp?"

Cass thought about the sensation in his jaw. The precise location of the wrongness. "Eighth of a tone. Maybe less."

The old man was quiet for a moment. He struck his blue-green thumb against his forefinger, a small percussive gesture, like testing a surface. "Most trained singers wouldn't catch an eighth. Not in market conditions."

"I'm not a trained singer."

"I know what you are. Bellwright boy. I've seen you come through this market before." He extended one stained hand. "Torvald Hess."

Cass shook it. "Cass."

"I know." Hess looked back at the cloth merchant's stall. "The woman in the apron is going to find out in about two weeks that the binding she thinks she has isn't what she thinks it is. By then the cloth merchant will have sold the stock. She'll file an appeal, if she can afford the fee, and the appeal will take four months, and by the time it resolves she'll have lost the season." He said all of this without particular bitterness, the way someone describes weather. "That's how it goes."

"Someone should have stopped the broker before she sang it."

"Someone with credentials should have."

"I don't have credentials."

"I know." Hess looked at him again. "You're at the Academy."

"Three more years."

"Three more years." He said it the way you'd say *a long time*. "The woman in the apron doesn't have three more years. She needed someone today."

Cass had no answer for that. He was aware of how young he sounded without having said anything.

"Come find me when you have those credentials," Hess said. He turned back toward the Weavers' District road. "I'm in the Tanner's Quarter most days. Ask for Hess — people know where I am." He paused. "You've got a good ear, boy. Better than good. Don't let them sand it down."

He walked away without looking back.

Cass stood at the market's edge for a moment. The cloth merchant was laughing at something a neighboring stall-keeper had said. The woman in the weaver's apron was folding her copy of the ratification record, tucking it into her coat with the careful satisfaction of someone who believed they had protected themselves.

He turned away before he had to keep watching it.

---

His father was at the lathe when Cass got back. He could hear the rhythm of it before he opened the gate — the pauses where Eddan stopped to check the pitch-gauge, the small adjustments that meant the work was going well. The workshop smelled of metal-dust and linseed oil, the faint char of the morning's forge work still in the air, and the bells in progress along the walls were humming faintly in sympathy with the lathe's vibration.

Cass set the flux on the supply shelf, labeled side out. He heard his father's hands pause on the lathe and then continue. That was acknowledgment enough.

He went through the connecting door into the house, meaning to drop his bag and come back to help with the secondary bell work.

There was a letter on the kitchen table.

Face-down. The paper was thin but stiff, cream-gray — the Corda compound's stock. He had seen four letters in the last year, each of them face-down when he found them.

He turned this one over.

Perin's handwriting was neat and very small, the handwriting of someone who had learned to fit a lot into a small space.

*The weather here has been cold and damp. The east wing resonance chamber needs re-tuning — the humidity has been affecting the wall panels, and the reference tone is running approximately two cents flat of true. I've submitted a maintenance request. They'll get to it eventually.*

Cass read that twice. Two cents flat. His father would know the number immediately — Eddan tracked the Corda compound's resonance chambers the way he tracked all the secondary installations in the city, as a professional habit, the way a carpenter noted which doors were warping. Two cents flat was significant. It meant every contract ratified in that chamber for the past several weeks had been built on a reference tone that was slightly, specifically wrong.

He kept reading.

*The new commission work is interesting from a technical standpoint. The broker they've brought in from Veth uses iron-bell references rather than bronze, which produces a slightly different harmonic envelope around the ratification intervals. I've been studying the difference. The Veth approach has some structural advantages in outdoor contract settings where bronze would be affected by temperature variance. I don't expect the Academy to find this interesting.*

*I had fish soup for lunch three days running. The cook here makes it well. I hope father's hands are better in the cold season. I hope your theory work is going. I hope the Seventh of Ash Street is running clean — it tends to drift in autumn, as you know.*

*P.*

Cass set it back face-down.

He stood in the kitchen with one hand flat on the table, the letter under his palm. Through the workshop wall he could hear the lathe, the pause, the pitch-gauge check, the lathe again.

He thought about what Perin had written. The specific, technical care of it — the resonance chamber's drift, the Veth broker's harmonics, the exact quality of the fish soup. Three observations that had nothing to do with each other. Or that had everything to do with each other, if you were paying attention to the right thing.

He picked the letter up again.

*The reference tone is running approximately two cents flat of true.*

*The Veth approach has some structural advantages in outdoor contract settings.*

*I hope the Seventh of Ash Street is running clean.*

Three things Perin would know Cass could verify. Three things that had specific, measurable values. The east wing chamber's drift, the Veth broker's harmonic envelope, the Seventh's autumn variance. All of them checkable. All of them things Perin could not say directly — not in a letter that left the Corda compound, not in writing that anyone could read.

He set the letter down. Face-down, as he'd found it.

He went back through the connecting door into the workshop. His father's hands were settled on the lathe, the tremor gone, as it always was in here, and the secondary bell on the frame was almost done — a clean, patient shape, a bell that would do its work for another generation if the alloy held.

"Letter came," Cass said.

"I saw it." His father's eyes stayed on the work.

"He says the east wing resonance chamber is running flat."

"Two cents. He'll have it sorted."

"Has it been two cents before?"

A pause. The lathe continued. "It drifts in humid weather."

"He says he's submitted a maintenance request."

"Then they'll see to it."

Cass watched his father's hands on the lathe. Steady. Absolutely steady, the way they never were at the dinner table.

"He says the Seventh drifts in autumn," Cass said.

"It always has." His father picked up the pitch-gauge and struck a test note.

The bell rang a clean D, true to center, and the tone spread through the workshop and touched the other bells on their frames and they answered it in their different voices, each one a slightly different shade of the same frequency. For a few seconds the workshop was full of sound going in all directions at once.

Cass closed his eyes. Counted the resonances. Let them settle.

He thought about the lame chord in the market. The way his jaw had caught the wrongness before his mind named it. The woman in the weaver's apron folding her ratification record into her coat.

He thought about Perin writing in small, careful handwriting about fish soup and humidity and iron bells.

He thought about Lenne Ashvale saying *the Codex doesn't say I'm wrong* and meaning it as an argument, and about himself saying *that's not what an argument is* and being right, and about both of them being right, in their different ways, about a gap the Codex had left open for a hundred and sixty years.

"Hand me the flux," his father said.

Cass got it from the shelf. Their fingers touched on the jar, and neither of them said anything, and the workshop held its warmth around them both, and the bells on their frames went on humming, very faintly, long after the struck note had stopped.
---

# Chapter Three: The Forty-Seven

The Eleventh of Cooper Lane came down on the rope-and-pulley in a slow, swinging arc, and Cass's job was to keep it from swinging.

"Left," his father said.

Cass pulled the guide rope left. The bell steadied. His father's hands found the frame and secured it with three practiced motions.

"Clapper first."

Cass was already reaching for the clapper puller. Long handle, notched end, built for this purpose and nothing else. He passed it over.

"You checked the Seventh last autumn," Cass said. "Before Perin's letter."

"I check the Seventh every autumn."

"So you knew it was drifting."

"I knew it was drifting." His father pulled the clapper free and held it to the light. His thumb found the crack at the crown without looking for it — just moved there, the way a tongue finds a loose tooth. "Knowing something's drifting and knowing how far are different problems."

Cass looked at the crack. Hairline, barely visible, running along the crown in a direction that told him everything about the bell's recent history. "Can it be patched?"

"You patch a crown crack, you change the weight distribution."

"Which changes the strike angle."

"Which changes the pitch." His father set the clapper on the workbench. "So you recast."

This was the conversation Cass liked. Not because the subject was interesting — he'd known crown cracks couldn't be patched since he was nine — but because this version of his father existed in a different register from the dinner-table version. Here, the answers came without hesitation. Here, the question was the right shape and the answer was the right shape and they fit together the way intervals fit.

No under-note at all. His father's voice in the workshop was the cleanest sound Cass knew — not just the absence of lying but the active presence of someone saying exactly what they meant, with no gap between the thought and the word. It was the only place Cass could listen without bracing.

He handed over the measurement gauge without being asked. His father took it.

"The Seventh's going to want more than a half-cent adjustment," Cass said. "It sounded a third flat to me this morning."

His father glanced up. Not a full look. A check.

"When the Civic Pitch ran," Cass said. "I was in the kitchen."

"The kitchen window faces west. You were hearing it off the wall."

"I know. It was still a third flat."

His father made a note in the ledger. He didn't say Cass was wrong, which was its own kind of answer.

---

The Seventh came down mid-morning, and they worked on it together for the better part of an hour. The process was slow and iterative — his father filing at the inner rim with a crescent tool, removing bronze in the specific places where removal would raise the pitch, and Cass working the reference tone with the old brass tuning fork, checking after each pass.

A bell was a ratio, his father had explained when Cass was small. Not a note. A ratio between thickness and diameter and the mineral content of the alloy, all three of them in conversation with each other, and you couldn't change one without the others responding. Tuning was listening to the conversation and adjusting until the bell said what you needed it to say.

"Major third," Cass said, after his father's third pass. He struck the fork, let the reference tone settle, then tapped the Seventh's rim. The two sounds met in the air.

"Flat side of a major third," his father said.

"I know. I'm saying we're close." He struck the fork again. "Major third is when two notes lock together. Like a handshake. Right now it's more like — a handshake where someone's gripping too hard."

His father lowered the crescent tool and looked at him.

"What?" Cass said.

"Where did you get that."

"I don't know. It sounds like that."

His father was quiet for a moment. Then he picked up the crescent tool and made another pass, lighter this time. "Strike it again."

Cass struck the fork. Let the reference tone sound. Tapped the Seventh.

The major third locked. Clean, settled, two notes agreeing with each other the way the word *yes* felt in the chest.

"There," his father said.

"There." Cass made the notation in the ledger. "That's the handshake."

His father set the crescent tool down and picked up the pitch-gauge to confirm. The gauge agreed. He made his own notation without comment, and Cass didn't push it, but he felt the thing between them in the workshop air — not approval exactly, something quieter and more durable than approval.

They moved to the Twenty-Third of the Weavers' Road. Lip deformation, slight, from a bad strike. Not affecting pitch yet.

"Will in a year," Cass said, looking at it.

"Eighteen months."

"Year and a half, then."

"That's what eighteen months means."

Cass handed him the inspection lamp. "I'm going to check the alloy stock while you're doing this."

His father didn't look up. "It's fine."

"I know." He went to the far wall anyway.

The old-alloy bronze sat on its separate shelf, darker than the standard casting bronze, with the faint reddish tinge that the standard material didn't have. Six ingots. Maybe seven if the partial at the back was more than it looked.

He stood there and counted them twice.

"There are six ingots," he said.

"Cass."

"Seven if the partial counts. It doesn't look like it counts."

His father's file moved against the Twenty-Third's lip with a sound like a slow exhale. "The stock is managed."

"Managed how? We've used three ingots in the last eight months. I've been watching."

"I know you've been watching."

"So how is it managed?"

The file stopped. His father set it down on the workbench with a careful, deliberate motion, the kind that meant he was choosing not to do something else with his hands. "The stock is sufficient for current needs. When it isn't, I'll make arrangements."

"What arrangements? There's no more of this alloy. It's not available. You've said that."

"I've said the source isn't documented."

"Which means you don't know where to get more."

"Which means I haven't needed to find out yet."

Cass looked at the six ingots on the shelf. He looked at his father's back. "The Thirty-Third of the Civic Square was repaired eight months ago. The Eighth of the Bell Quarter Road was repaired in spring. Both from old-alloy stock. If either of them needs attention in the next three years —"

"Cass." His father picked up the file again. "Hand me the pitch-gauge."

Cass got it from the hook. He crossed the workshop and held it out. His father took it without turning around, and his hands were steady, absolutely steady, and that was how Cass knew he'd pushed as far as pushing would get him today.

He went back to his stool.

The file resumed. The Twenty-Third gave up its deformation degree by degree. Outside, the Bell Quarter went about its morning — the distant sound of the bronze-dealers' carts on the lane, a conversation that carried from somewhere up the slope and then didn't. The forge at the workshop's far end ticked as it cooled.

After a while, without looking up from the bell, his father said: "A perfect fifth is a promise."

Cass waited.

"Two notes, five steps apart. When they sound together, they feel like something that ought to continue. Like a sentence with the period missing." His father checked the gauge. Made a small adjustment. "That's why we use it in the binding sequence. A descending fifth from the ratification note. It resolves downward, which means it completes. The promise lands."

"I know what a descending fifth does."

"I know you do. I'm telling you why." His father set the gauge down. "Your grandfather used to say the intervals aren't symbols. They're physics. The major third locks because of the frequency ratio — five to four, two notes that share enough overtones to agree with each other. The fifth is three to two. The octave is two to one, which is why it sounds like the same note — it is the same note, just doubled. None of it is metaphor."

Cass thought about this. "Then what's a lame chord?"

"A lame chord is when the intervals are almost right." His father turned to look at him properly for the first time all morning. "Close enough to pass a quick hearing. Wrong enough that the binding doesn't hold the way it should. One party's note lands in a slightly different key than the other's. They think they've agreed. They haven't, quite." He paused. "Like a lock where the key turns but doesn't catch. You think the door is locked. It isn't."

"And the Academy can detect it?"

"A trained Court Singer can detect it. If they're listening for it." His father picked up the file again. "If they're not, a lame chord can go on the record as valid."

Cass sat with that for a moment. "How often does that happen?"

His father didn't answer. He went back to the Twenty-Third, and the file moved against the bronze, and the workshop held its warmth around them, and Cass didn't ask again.

---

Forty-seven steps to the bell chamber. Cass counted them without deciding to.

The tower was cold after the workshop — stone cold, the kind that had been accumulating since autumn and wouldn't release until spring. His breath came out visible. His father's too, two small clouds rising in the stairwell as they climbed, the inspection lamp throwing their shadows long and irregular against the curved walls.

The bell chamber opened at the top.

Cass had been here dozens of times. He always had to stop himself from stopping in the doorway. The chamber was vaulted limestone, the ceiling high enough that sounds took a moment to return from it, the four arched windows open to the afternoon and the cold air moving through them with a sound like breath across the mouth of a jar. The forty-seven original bells hung from Aldric Bellwright's iron frames, and they were — present, was the word Cass always came back to. Not large, though some of them were. Present. The bronze of the originals was darker and finer-grained than the secondary bells he'd spent the morning with, and even in the cold and the quiet they held something in the air around them, a latent readiness, the way a tuning fork held in the hand still vibrated after the sound had gone.

He went directly to Bell Four and began the inspection.

Strike with the padded mallet — barely a tap, just enough to set the metal speaking. Listen. Check the gauge. Record. Move on.

Bell Four. Bell Five. Bell Six.

The work settled him. It always did. The methodical movement from bell to bell, the small satisfaction of each entry in the ledger, the way the chamber's acoustics made even the soft inspection taps carry and return. His father worked from the other end, starting at Bell One, and the two of them would meet somewhere in the middle.

Bell Eleven. Bell Twelve.

The afternoon light through the west window made a long rectangle on the stone floor. He watched it from the corner of his eye as he worked, the way it moved almost imperceptibly, the sun tracking across the rim of the bowl.

Bell Fifteen. Bell Sixteen.

He was thinking about the lame chord. The woman in the weaver's apron from the market two days ago, the way his jaw had tightened before his mind had caught up. The sound of a key that turned without catching. He was thinking about Lenne Ashvale's voice in the corridor: *the Codex doesn't say I'm wrong.* He was thinking about the old-alloy stock, six ingots on a shelf, his father saying *the stock is managed* with hands that had gone too still.

He struck Bell Nineteen.

The bell spoke — a clear mid-range note, one of the smaller originals, the sound it had been making for two hundred years. And then, beneath it —

Not a sound. Not exactly. Something that arrived before sound, before his ears had finished processing the bell's note. A pressure in his chest like someone leaning on him from inside. Not pain — it was nowhere near pain — but weight, the specific weight of something large and patient, something that had been there long before he walked into the room and would be there long after.

He stood still.

The bell's note dissolved. The pressure didn't. It was in his sternum, in the bones of his ribcage, a resonance that felt like — he couldn't get his mind around it. Not a note. Not the under-note. Always wrong, always the frequency of a mouth saying what a throat refused. This was the opposite. This was a frequency so settled in itself that it made everything around it suddenly aware of its own pitch, the way a struck tuning fork made the air in a room suddenly audible.

His body had gone quiet. Not the effortful quiet he held in loud spaces, the kind that took work. The kind that happened to him. Like the workshop after a clean bell note, that six-second pool of sound — only this didn't stop. It sat in his chest and waited.

He didn't know how long he stood there. Long enough that the rectangle of light on the stone floor had shifted.

"You heard it."

His father's voice. Not a question.

Cass turned. Eddan stood near Bell Thirty with the ledger open in his hands, watching him. The expression on his face was the one Cass hadn't been able to name in the street afterward, the one that came back to him now with its name: recognition. The look of a man who has been waiting for a specific thing and has watched it arrive.

The chamber was very quiet.

"How long have you known?" Cass said.

His father looked down at the ledger. He made a notation. He moved to Bell Thirty-One. "We have fourteen bells left on the rotation."

Cass stood with the mallet in his hand and the pressure still in his chest, patient and enormous, and looked at his father's back.

"That's not what I asked."

"No." His father's voice was flat. Not unkind. Flat the way a door is flat. "It isn't. Strike Bell Nineteen and write down what the gauge says."

Cass raised the mallet. His hand was steady. He struck the bell, and the note rang out, and beneath it the pressure deepened for a moment, a single breath of something that had no name in the Interval Codex, and then the bell's note dissolved and the pressure settled back to its resting state, which was still there, which was still waiting.

He wrote down what the gauge said.

---

They left the tower as the evening bells began in the outer districts. The custodian marked their exit without looking up. The street outside was cooler, the sun gone behind the rim.

His father walked half a step ahead. The ledger was under his arm. His hands were in his coat pockets.

Cass waited until they reached the quieter lane, the bronze-dealers shuttered for the evening, a cat on a windowsill watching them with the particular disinterest of animals that had heard too many important conversations to be impressed.

"What did Aldric Bellwright put in the bells?"

His father stopped. Both feet on the pavement, facing slightly away, as if he'd paused to look at something that wasn't there.

"I know the official record," Cass said. "I know the Civic Pitch sequence and the reference tone and the Codification history. I don't mean that."

His father's shoulders moved. Something small.

"The harmonic. The thing underneath." Cass heard his own voice come out steadier than he'd expected. "I've been hearing it for six months in the mornings. Today in the chamber I heard it clearly. You watched me hear it and you said *we have fourteen bells left on the rotation.*"

The lane was quiet. Somewhere in the distance, the Seventh sounded its close-of-day strike — a clean D, the one they'd tuned that morning, and Cass felt the small involuntary satisfaction of it and then it was gone and his father still hadn't moved.

"Your hands shake worst on mornings when the Civic Pitch runs long," Cass said. "I've noticed that for two years."

Eddan pulled his hands from his pockets. The tremor was visible — not the fine tremor of the dinner table, more than that, a tremor that had used up whatever the workshop's peace had given it and found nothing left in reserve. He looked at his hands with the expression of a man whose body has said something without his permission.

"The bells do what they've always done," he said. "That's enough."

He walked on.

Cass stood in the lane and watched his father's back. The set of his shoulders. The slight unevenness of his gait that had nothing to do with his legs. The ledger under his arm, all forty-seven entries, everything documented and correct and telling nothing.

*The bells do what they've always done.*

Physics, his father had said that morning. Not metaphor. The major third locks because of the frequency ratio, five to four, two notes sharing enough overtones to agree. None of it is metaphor.

And yet here was his father walking away from a question with an answer that was the shape of a door, and the pressure was still in Cass's chest, patient and enormous, and it didn't feel like nothing. It felt like a question asked in a language he hadn't learned yet. A different thing from a question with no answer.

The cat watched him from the windowsill. He looked back. It looked away first.

He walked home.

---

The house was quiet by the time he went to bed. The lathe had started up after dinner — that low familiar vibration through the kitchen wall — and Cass had washed the dishes and stood in the dark kitchen for a moment before going upstairs.

The locked room was off the kitchen. He'd stood near it for a moment, not touching the door, just being in the room where it was.

He lay in his bed and looked at the ceiling.

The pressure from the bell chamber had faded with distance, the way the Civic Pitch faded as you walked toward the rim. But the memory of it was precise. Not a sound. A weight in the chest, patient, like something that had been there long before the Bellwright family and would be there after. And his father's face in the chamber — not surprised, not afraid, or not only afraid — watching Cass go still among the bells with the expression of a man who had been expecting this for a long time and had hoped anyway that it wouldn't come.

*You heard it.* Not a question. The father recognizing the moment.

Which meant Eddan had heard it too. Or had heard of it. Or had read about it somewhere, in something sealed and locked, in a room off the kitchen with three bells Cass had never been allowed to touch.

The lathe ran on below him. The house held its warmth. Cass lay still and thought about six ingots of old-alloy bronze on a shelf, about a lame chord that looked like an agreement but wasn't, about the blank pages at the back of Volume Four that had been blank for a hundred and sixty years because nobody was willing to write in them.

*The bells do what they've always done.*

His father believed that. Or had trained himself to believe it, which was not the same thing, and Cass could hear the difference even when it wasn't a sound.

He didn't sleep for a long time.
---

# Chapter Four: The Codex and the Summons

The Academy library smelled of binding glue and old paper and the close, dry air of a room where windows were kept shut to protect the manuscripts. The silence in here was different from the workshop's silence. The workshop held the memory of the lathe, the cooling tick of bronze. The library's silence was enforced — the shelves absorbed what little noise there was and gave nothing back.

He had forty minutes before afternoon theory.

The official founding record took up two pages in the Civic Index. Aldric Bellwright, bronze-caster and amateur acoustician, settled in the limestone bowl with a guild of craftsmen. Spent eleven years casting forty-seven bells from a proprietary alloy. Composed the Civic Pitch sequence. Taught it to his guild. The system spread because it worked. Clean and thin, the record told him almost nothing he didn't already know.

He moved to the academic papers.

Three dealt with the harmonic structure of the original bells — not the sequence itself but the underlying mathematics of how Aldric had tuned them. A fourth line in the catalog index read: *Fen, M. — Structural Analysis of Foundational Pitch Variance — WITHDRAWN.* No shelf location. He filed it away. Two required a request from the restricted shelf, which meant signing his name in a ledger and waiting while the librarian's assistant climbed a ladder. He used the time to read the third, which turned out to be inconclusive in the specific way of scholarship that had run out of evidence before it ran out of confidence. A man named Priel, forty years dead, had measured the bells' overtone series and found that the intervals exceeded standard temperament by a factor he couldn't account for. He had concluded, without apparent embarrassment, that this was probably a casting error incorporated into the system by accident, and that the system worked despite it.

Cass set the paper aside.

The restricted papers arrived. The librarian's assistant — a boy two years younger than Cass, collar buttoned too high — set them on the table with the careful neutrality of someone who wanted to ask what they were for and had been trained not to. Cass thanked him. The boy retreated.

The second paper was better. A woman named Aldeth Sour, writing sixty years ago, had approached the problem from the receiving end: not the bells' construction but their effect on listeners in the Civic Square. She had documented that listeners standing within forty feet of the Bell Tower during the Civic Pitch experienced a measurable increase in what she called *sympathetic resonance* — a vibration in the chest cavity that persisted for several minutes after the sequence ended. She had concluded this was a property of the limestone bowl's natural amplification and had nothing to do with the bells' specific tuning.

She had been wrong, Cass suspected. But usefully wrong. She had measured the right thing and drawn the wrong conclusion from it.

He wrote *chest resonance — limestone or bell structure?* in the margin and turned to the third paper.

The name registered slowly. *Korven Voss, Lecturer in Acoustic Theory, Cantamura Academy of Civic Harmony.* He thought of Senna Voss, the throat-remedy woman in the Tanner's Quarter. He filed the question and kept reading.

For ten pages, Korven Voss had been doing something extraordinary. He had started with the overtone series Priel had dismissed as a casting error and traced it back — not to the bells' physical dimensions, the standard approach, but to the alloy itself. He had gotten a sample of the old bronze from somewhere and had it analyzed, and what he found was that the alloy contained a trace mineral compound that altered how the metal vibrated at the fundamental frequency. Not just at the fundamental. At the sub-fundamental. Below the range of the note itself, below the range of the instrument, below anything a trained ear should be able to detect, the alloy produced a second vibration. Not an overtone. An undertone. Something that ran beneath the bell's voice the way a river ran beneath ice — present, moving, invisible from above.

Voss had been building toward something. Page ten ended mid-sentence: *—which would suggest that Aldric's original composition was not simply a reference sequence but a structured harmonic that, when heard in its complete form, would produce in any listener with sufficient sensitivity a—*

Page eleven was blank.

Pages twelve through thirty were blank.

Cass turned them one at a time. The paper was old, edges slightly foxed, but the blankness was not decay. The pages had never been written on. He held one up to the light from the high window. No impression from a pen. No ghost of text. Nothing.

He set it down.

He was about to close the folder when his eye caught the catalog slip tucked inside the back cover — a standard Academy record, listing the paper's acquisition date, its classification, its restrictions. At the bottom, under a column marked *cross-references*, two entries. The first was the Priel paper he'd already read. The second was a single line: *M. Fen, unpublished, withdrawn.*

No title. No date. No subject notation. Just the name and those two words.

He stared at it for a moment. *Withdrawn* was different from *restricted*. Restricted meant someone had decided access should be limited. Withdrawn meant someone had pulled it from the record entirely — and then, for some reason, left the cross-reference behind.

He wrote the name in his notes. Then he gathered his papers, returned the restricted documents to the desk, and went to find a practice room.

---

The practice rooms in the east wing were small and close-walled, each one lined with acoustic panels meant to prevent sound from bleeding between rooms. They mostly worked. From the next room, faint but clear, someone was running scales — the same ascending figure, over and over, with a slight drag on the sixth.

Cass sat at the small writing table and spread his notes.

He tried to write down the harmonic from the Bell Tower. Standard notation gave him five ledger lines and four spaces. He wrote the F below the bass clef staff — the lowest note the Codex recognized as usable in a binding sequence. The harmonic sat below that. Not slightly below. Not a quarter-tone, the way the under-note did. This was different. Below the staff, below the ledger lines, below the range of the lowest bell in the civic sequence, below the range of anything he had been taught to name.

He wrote the F. He drew a downward arrow beneath it. He wrote: *below F.*

Two words. Accurate and useless, like a map that only showed you something existed past the edge.

"What is that."

He turned.

Lenne Ashvale stood in the doorway with her composition folder under one arm and her copper practice sash over her shoulder. She was looking at his notation with her head tilted slightly, the way she did when something didn't resolve as expected.

"I've been looking for you," she said. "The contract exercise — we were supposed to work through the third-clause structures together. I asked Devin but he composes like he's afraid of the interval."

"Sorry. I was in the library."

She came in and set her folder on the table. She looked at the note on the page. In the next room, the student dragged the sixth again.

"Your father asked you to work on this?"

"I'm working on something for him." Which was true in the sense that the Voss paper's blank pages were true — accurate as far as it went.

She looked at the notation another moment. Then she sat down across from him, pulled the page toward her, and studied it with the focused quiet she brought to anything that didn't resolve immediately.

"That's below the bass staff," she said.

"Yes."

"The Codex bottoms out at the F below the staff. Aldric's second bell." She pushed the page back. "So this is either a transcription error or something that doesn't exist."

"It exists. I heard it."

She looked at him. He looked back. Her eyes moved slightly — tracking intervals in her head, fingering through progressions against her thumb.

"Where?"

"Bell Tower. Three days ago."

"The Bell Tower has forty-seven bells. The lowest is the Second Bell of the Founding sequence, tuned to—"

"E-flat, yes, I know. This was below that."

"By how much?"

Cass thought about it. "I don't have the vocabulary. That's the problem."

Lenne was quiet for a moment. The sixth dragged in the next room.

"Volume Four," she said. "Has blank pages at the back." She said it the way she might say *the binding requires two voices* — a piece of information being set on the table.

"I know."

"Forty-three of them. I counted once. In the third year, when we study the exceptional circumstances chapter. I counted because the chapter ends mid-argument and I wanted to know how much space was left before the back cover." She paused. "Mid-argument. Which argument?"

"The one about frequencies that fall outside codified intervals. The chapter says" — she opened her composition folder, turned past the exercises, and found a page of her own notes — "*any frequency not codified in Volumes One through Three exists in a state of acoustic ambiguity, pending formal recognition by the Academy's Committee on Interval Expansion.* Then a footnote. Then the chapter ends."

"When was the Committee on Interval Expansion last convened?"

She looked up. "I don't know that it was."

"It wasn't. Not once in a hundred and sixty years."

She absorbed this. In the next room, the ascending scale stopped.

"So the Committee exists on paper," she said.

"The Committee exists on paper, Volume Four has forty-three blank pages, and the Voss paper stops mid-sentence on page ten." He spread his notes. "Korven Voss. Forty years ago. Lecturer in Acoustic Theory. He found an undertone in the old bronze alloy — not an overtone, an undertone, below the bell's fundamental — and he was building an argument about what it meant when the paper just ends. Thirty blank pages."

Lenne looked at the notes. She reached across and turned the page with the downward arrow toward her.

"*Below F,*" she read.

"I know."

"That's your argument? *Below F?*"

"That's all I have."

She sat back. She was doing the composing-face again — not quite a frown, more like the expression of someone listening to a chord that hadn't resolved yet.

"Voss is in the restricted collection?"

"The last twenty pages are blank. Someone restricted a paper with thirty blank pages."

"That's not nothing," she said. "That's someone deciding the blank pages needed restricting."

Cass looked at her.

"What," she said.

"I didn't think of it that way."

"The content isn't dangerous. The content is nothing. So the restriction is about the question, not the answer." She picked up her pen and wrote something in the margin of her notes — small, precise. "Which means whoever restricted it knew there was an answer and didn't want people looking for it."

"Or knew there was an answer and was afraid of it."

"Those are the same thing."

"No they're not," Cass said.

She looked up. "Explain."

"Afraid of it means they know what it is. Didn't want people looking means they might not. One of those is a cover-up and one is just — institutional caution."

"Rector Suvaine has blocked three attempts to complete Volume Four," Lenne said. "Is that afraid of it or institutional caution?"

"I don't know yet."

She made a small sound. Not quite skeptical. Something more like *fair enough.*

"There was a catalog cross-reference," Cass said. "In the Voss paper's folder. *M. Fen, unpublished, withdrawn.* No title. No date. Just the name."

Lenne was still. "Withdrawn is different from restricted."

"Someone pulled it from the record and then left the reference behind. Either they forgot or they wanted someone to find it."

"Or they were interrupted."

He hadn't thought of that. He wrote *M. Fen* at the top of a fresh page and put it aside.

"The blank pages in Volume Four," she said. "You think they're connected to the blank pages in the Voss paper."

"I think Voss was writing toward something that would have filled some of them."

"And the Bell Tower harmonic is the thing he was writing toward."

"I think it's related. I don't know how."

She turned her pen over in her fingers, once. "Does your father know you're looking at this?"

"He knows I heard something in the Bell Tower." Cass paused. "He didn't seem surprised."

She was quiet for a moment. Then she said: "My mother sang the Merchant Quarter verdict last spring. I watched from the gallery." She turned a page in the folder without reading it. "The form was perfect. Every interval correct. I've been thinking about it since."

"What was the verdict?"

"A debt dispute. The creditor won. The debtor was a man from the Weavers' District who couldn't afford a song-broker to review the original contract, so he'd ratified it himself, and there was a lame chord in the third clause that he didn't catch. It held him without holding the creditor." She paused. "The verdict was correctly sung. That's not what I've been thinking about."

Cass waited.
