"The form being correct doesn't tell you anything about whether the original contract was just. The verdict was correctly sung. The original contract had a lame chord. The man lost his trade license." She set her pen down. "The system worked exactly as designed. I don't know what to do with that."

"Neither do I. But I think it's the same question."

She looked at him.

"The blank pages," he said. "The Voss paper. The Bell Tower harmonic. They're all the same question. The Codex has edges, and something exists past them, and nobody has written it down, and the people who know that have decided not to."

"That's a very large claim."

"I know."

"You heard one sound in the Bell Tower."

"I've been hearing sounds that aren't in the Codex for four years," Cass said. "The under-note." He watched her face. "You know what that is."

"I've heard students use the term," she said carefully.

"It's not a student term. It's what I call the frequency I hear when someone sings a lie. Or sings something they've been forced to sing. It sits between F and F-sharp, a quarter-tone that doesn't exist in any scale the Academy teaches, and it hurts." He said it flatly. "It's been hurting since I was ten. The Bell Tower harmonic is different — it doesn't hurt, it's lower, it's older — but it's the same kind of thing. Something that exists outside the Codex."

Lenne was looking at him with an expression he couldn't read.

"You hear it every time someone lies," she said.

"Every time someone sings a lie. Or a coerced truth. There's a difference in texture."

"And you've never told anyone."

"My brother knew. Knows." He picked up his pen. "It's not useful information. I can't prove it. The Court Singer validates the oath as clean and that's the record."

"But you know."

"I know."

She was quiet for a long moment. He could see her working through something — not with the composing-face, something more careful.

"The man from the Weavers' District," she said finally. "If you had been in that courtroom. When my mother sang the verdict."

"I would have heard whether the original contract had a lame chord, yes. If anyone sang it aloud."

"And if they had?"

"I still couldn't prove it."

She picked up her pen. She looked at the exercise page. Then she put the pen down again.

"The third-clause exercises," she said. "Can we actually do them now, or are you still thinking about the blank pages?"

"I'm always thinking about the blank pages. I can do both."

She made a dry sound — barely a breath. "Right. Show me the problem with Devin's approach."

"He resolves to the tonic when the clause calls for a suspended fourth."

"The tonic is correct per the Codex."

"The tonic closes the binding before the third clause has been stated. The whole thing becomes provisional." He pulled the exercise sheet toward him. "It's technically correct and functionally wrong."

"The Codex says tonic resolution after the second clause."

"The Codex assumes the second clause is complete. Look at the exercise — the second clause has a conditional embedded in it. If the condition hasn't been met, the clause is still open. You can't resolve to the tonic off an open clause."

She leaned over to look at the exercise. He could see her reading it properly this time.

"The conditional is in the subordinate phrase," she said slowly.

"Second measure. After the ratification."

"The Codex notation doesn't mark it as a conditional."

"It's marked with a minor seventh — the unstable interval, the one that has to resolve within the same breath. When you see an unresolved minor seventh in a subordinate phrase, the clause is open."

She took the exercise sheet and read the second measure again. "I didn't catch that."

"It's in the third line."

"Devin definitely didn't catch it."

"Devin doesn't read subordinate phrases. He goes straight to the resolution."

She wrote *unresolved m7 = open clause* in her margin with two underlines. "How did you know to look for it?"

"The header says the parties are in a ten-foot resonance chamber. A fifth at that ceiling height creates a standing wave that interferes with the third clause. You'd need a fourth instead. The fifth tells you something's wrong with the standard approach, which means you look harder at the clauses."

"The Codex says fifth."

"The Codex footnotes say fourteen feet minimum for a standard chamber. Chapter Seven, footnote thirty-one."

She wrote *Ch. 7, fn. 31* in the margin. "I didn't read the footnotes."

"The footnotes are where they put the things they want you to miss."

"That's a very cynical way to read a legal textbook."

"It's worked so far."

She looked at him for a moment. Then she looked back at the exercise. "So the correct approach is — fourth instead of fifth at the second-clause resolution, which keeps the third clause open, which means the tonic can't come until after the third clause completes."

"Right."

"Which means Devin's contracts, if anyone ever actually used them, would be provisional from the second clause forward."

"If the ceiling was ten feet, yes."

She turned the exercise sheet over in her hands. "Did you catch it the first time?"

"No. I caught the standing-wave problem and worked backward."

"I would have caught it if I'd read the footnotes."

"Probably."

"I always read the footnotes," she said, with a slight edge that was almost offense.

"You didn't read them this time."

"I was looking for you." She said it flatly, without embarrassment, and moved on. "Show me the fourth-instead-of-fifth substitution. I want to see how it changes the third clause structure."

They worked through the remaining exercises. His shoulders loosened without him noticing. The room settled into the quiet of two people working in parallel, close enough to consult, far enough not to disturb. The student in the next room finally corrected the sixth, ran the scale clean twice, and stopped.

She caught an error in the fifth exercise that he had missed — a codicil amendment that extended past the breath limit, which made it legally void under standard form.

"How long is a trained singer's breath?" he asked.

She looked up. "Four seconds minimum, per the Codex."

"Has anyone measured it? Actually measured it?"

"That's what the Codex says."

He looked at the exercise. "Korven Voss and Senna Voss. The throat-remedy woman in the Tanner's Quarter. Her remedies work — my brother used them. If she's related to Korven, she might know something about the paper."

Lenne was quiet for a moment. "Her mother uses them before high-stakes hearings. The Academy ones don't do anything." She paused. "If the answer to the blank pages isn't in the Academy, that would make sense. Someone decided not to put it there."

"The Compact uses folk intervals," Cass said. "Torvald Hess — the dye-merchant, the one who runs the outer-district dispute system — he's been settling contracts with uncodified intervals for twenty years. The Academy says they don't count. They work anyway."

"My mother's cases. He comes up." She didn't look up from the exercise. "The folk intervals work because the acoustic principles don't care about the Codex. The Codex is a record of what people have named. It's not a record of what exists."

"You should write that down."

"I did write it down. In a different notebook." She looked up. "The blank pages aren't the end of the Codex. They're the beginning of the part no one has been willing to write."

The afternoon light had shifted, going amber through the high window. He had not thought about the Voss paper for forty minutes.

He didn't name that. He gathered his papers and she gathered hers, and they went into the corridor without deciding to leave at the same time.

---

The corridor between the east wing and the main building carried sound oddly — a conversation at the south arcade came through clearly at the north end, something about the angle of the archways and how the limestone bounced. Cass had learned to walk it quietly.

He was halfway down it when he heard Rector Suvaine's voice.

She was in the north arcade, speaking to someone — one of the senior faculty, a man whose voice didn't carry as well. Cass slowed. Not because he meant to listen. Because the Rector's voice had a quality in formal settings that made his ear sharpen automatically, the way it did near anything that might produce an under-note.

"—entirely inappropriate use of the restricted collection. The Bellwright family journals have been sealed since the second generation. The request was flagged because the phrasing was specific enough to suggest prior knowledge of the contents."

The faculty member said something low and indistinct.

"The student is not the concern," Suvaine said. "The concern is where the prior knowledge originated. Someone has been talking about the journals to someone who should not have heard about them."

Cass stopped walking.

He stood in the corridor and waited. He listened to Rector Suvaine talk about a student who had been asking questions about the Bellwright family's sealed journals, and he held his breath, and he waited.

It didn't come.

He waited longer. His jaw was tight enough to ache. He listened to her voice — the cadence of it, the specific weight she put on *sealed* and *prior knowledge*, how her sentences resolved fully, no trailing edge, no hesitation. He waited for the quarter-tone between F and F-sharp, the greasy wrongness, the frequency that announced itself like a split lip.

Nothing.

A lie would have meant she was hiding something she knew. This meant she had been told something and had no reason to question it. She was looking for a student who had been asking about the journals. She was not lying. She was not afraid. She was a woman who had been handed a piece of information by someone who understood exactly what she would do with it, and she was doing it.

He was almost certain she was talking about him.

The faculty member said something. Suvaine's voice moved — she was turning, heading toward the main building. Cass walked. Not fast. The pace of a student going from one room to another, which was what he was.

He went through the main building and out the front entrance and down the steps into the late afternoon. The Civic Square was settling toward its end-of-day register — market stalls closing, the last contracts of the afternoon being ratified, the sound of the city going quieter as the light went.

He walked home through the Bell Quarter.

The workshop light was already on, amber behind the high window. His father had come down early. Cass stopped outside the front door and heard, faintly, the lathe running from the back of the building — the low, regular vibration of it coming through the stone wall, the sound that meant his father was somewhere the tremor couldn't reach him.

He went inside.

The kitchen was dim. The fire had been banked but not stoked, which meant his father had been in the workshop since before supper and hadn't come up. The bread from this morning was still on the board, half a loaf, and the cheese beside it had dried at the cut edge.

On the table, between the bread board and the cold lamp, was a letter.

Not a letter Cass had left there. Not a bill from the Academy, not a supply order from the bronze merchants. The seal on the back was a circle with a tuning fork inside it, rendered in dark green wax, and Cass had seen that seal precisely once before — on the contract his father had brought home ten years ago and locked in the room off the kitchen without ever explaining what it said.

The House of Corda.

He stood in the kitchen doorway for a moment. The lathe ran on through the wall. The bread sat on the board. The letter sat on the table.

He crossed the kitchen and picked it up.

The seal was already broken. His father had read it.

He read it.

*To the Bellwright Family, Bell Quarter, Cantamura — Greetings from the House of Corda.*

*This letter serves as formal notice of a scheduled Maintenance Assessment of the resonance chambers located within the Corda compound, as required under Section Four of the Bellwright Service Agreement. The Assessment is scheduled for the fourteenth day of the coming month. The family is requested to provide one qualified bell-wright for the duration of the Assessment, estimated at two days.*

*Per the terms of the Agreement, this request constitutes a standard obligation under the maintenance provisions and requires no additional ratification.*

*The House of Corda further notes, with appreciation, the continued fitness for service of the current Bellwright composer, whose work has been of exceptional quality throughout the term of engagement. We anticipate his continued contribution.*

*Signed in proper form, Maret Corda, House of Corda.*

Cass read it twice.

*Fitness for service.*

He set the letter down on the table and looked at it. The lathe ran on through the wall. He listened to it for a moment — the low, patient vibration of his father working through something he didn't want to name.

*Fitness for service.* Like Perin was a bell. Like Perin was something you assessed for cracks and tuned and put back on the frame.

He picked the letter up again and read the third paragraph for the third time. *Continued fitness.* Present tense. Not *has been fit* or *was fit* but *is fit*, the kind of language that assumed an ongoing state, an arrangement that had no expected end.

Perin's contract was twelve years. Everyone in the family knew that. Twelve years, and Perin had been gone for ten.

He set the letter down again. He stood at the kitchen table and counted back from now, and the arithmetic was simple, and the arithmetic was wrong.

Twelve years minus ten years was two years. In two years, Perin came home.

So why did the letter read like there was no end in sight?

He was still standing there when the lathe stopped.

He heard his father's footsteps in the workshop — the particular gait of a man who had been standing at the lathe for three hours, careful and slightly stiff, coming toward the kitchen door. He heard the workshop door open and his father's boots on the stone passage, and then the kitchen door, and then his father was standing in the kitchen with bronze dust on his hands and his apron and the particular flatness in his face that meant he had been expecting this moment and had been hoping it wouldn't arrive.

He looked at Cass. He looked at the letter on the table. He looked at Cass again.

"You read it," he said.

"It was open."

"I know." His father went to the basin and began washing his hands. His back was to the room. The water ran.

Cass waited.

"The assessment is —" His father paused. "Routine. The chambers need —" He stopped. "Someone has to go."

"You're going to go."

"Yes."

"You go every quarter," Cass said. "The maintenance reports. I know."

His father turned off the water. He dried his hands on the hanging cloth by the basin. He turned around, and his face was the face of a man who had been deciding how much to say for ten years and had still not finished deciding.

"Dad."

"Cass."

"The contract is twelve years." He kept his voice level. Flat. The same pitch he used when he was reading an interval and wanted to be accurate. "Perin signed ten years ago."

His father said nothing.

"The letter says *continued fitness for service.* That's present tense. That's not language for something that's ending in two years."

"Contract language is formal. It doesn't mean—"

"Which key was the original ratification sung in?"

His father stopped.

Cass watched him. The kitchen was very quiet. The banked fire made no sound. Outside, somewhere in the Bell Quarter, a cart went past on the stone lane, and then it was quiet again.

"I'm not asking to argue," Cass said. "I'm asking because it matters. Which key."

His father pulled out the chair at the head of the table and sat down. He put his hands flat on the table, and Cass could see them — the tremor was there, faint but present, the slight movement in the fingers that was always worse away from the lathe.

"G-major," he said. "Standard form."

"And the agreement was twelve years."

"Yes."

"So in two years—"

"The agreement was twelve years," his father said. "The contract is ten."

Cass heard it. He stood at the table and heard the sentence, and his , and he waited for the under-note, for the quarter-tone between F and F-sharp, for the proof that his father was lying.

It didn't come.

His father was not lying. His father had just said something true.

"The agreement," Cass said carefully, "and the contract are different things."

"Yes."

"Perin signed a contract for ten years. But there was an agreement — a different document — for twelve."

"The agreement predates the contract. It was — it was the terms Maret Corda set before the contract was composed. The broader terms. The contract was the ratified version, the one that was sung. The agreement was the — the framework."

"The framework that says twelve years."

"Yes."

"And the contract says ten."

"Yes."

Cass looked at his father's hands on the table. The tremor in the fingers. The bronze dust still caught in the creases of his knuckles where the water hadn't reached.

"So which one governs?" he said. "If the contract says ten years and the agreement says twelve — which one is binding?"

His father said nothing.

"The sung contract governs," Cass said. "That's Tonal Law. The ratified version supersedes any prior written framework. A sung contract in proper form in G-major, held for a full breath cycle, witnessed and registered — that's the binding document. That's what the Codex says."

"Yes," his father said.

"So the contract says ten years. In two years, Perin comes home."

His father looked at his hands.

"Unless," Cass said, "the contract has codicils."

The word sat in the kitchen air. His father's hands didn't move.

"Codicil amendments," Cass said. "A minor seventh added to the original binding note, resolved within the same breath. You can extend a contract's term with a codicil. You can extend it multiple times." He pulled out the chair across from his father and sat down. "How many codicils does the contract have?"

"Cass."

"How many."

His father looked up. His eyes were tired in a way that had nothing to do with three hours at the lathe.

"I don't know," he said.

"You don't know."

"I haven't seen the contract in ten years. It's held by the House of Corda. I have the — I have a copy of the original ratification. I don't have the amendments."

"You don't have the amendments," Cass said. The words came out very flat. "Perin signed a contract ten years ago and you don't know how long it actually runs."

"The agreement was twelve years. Maret assured me—"

"Maret Corda assured you." Cass heard his own voice. He didn't raise it. He didn't need to. "In writing? In a sung ratification? Or did she just tell you, and you believed her, and you've been counting down to twelve years ever since?"

His father's hands pressed flat against the table.

"She assured you," Cass said. "And in the meantime, the contract has codicils you haven't read, and the letter uses present-tense language about Perin's *continued fitness for service*, and you go to the Corda compound every quarter to file maintenance reports and you haven't asked to see the contract." He paused. "Have you."

The fire made a small sound. The coal settling.

"No," his father said.

Cass sat with that.

His father's hands were shaking. Not the faint tremor from before — more than that, a visible thing, the fingers pressing harder against the table as if the pressure could stop it. He was looking at the letter. Not at Cass. At the letter on the table between them, as if it were something he'd known was coming and had been hoping wouldn't arrive in this particular form.

"Why didn't you tell me," Cass said.

"You were four years old."

"I'm fourteen."

His father said nothing.

"I'm fourteen," Cass said again. "Perin's been gone ten years. I've been asking questions about the contract since I was old enough to understand what a contract was, and you've told me nothing except that he's well and he writes and the contract runs twelve years." He picked up the letter from the table. "*Continued fitness for service.* That's what they wrote. He's been gone ten years and they're writing letters about his fitness like he's a piece of equipment." He set it back down. "I needed to know this."

"I know," his father said.

"Then why—"

"Because I didn't want you to—" He stopped. He pressed his hands flat again. "I didn't want you to know what I did."

The kitchen was very quiet.

Cass looked at his father. The bronze dust in the knuckle-creases. The shaking hands. The tired eyes that had been tired for ten years, tired in the specific way of someone who has been carrying a thing alone and has stopped expecting it to get lighter.

"What did you do," Cass said.

His father looked at the letter.

"What did you do," Cass said again, and this time it was a question with a shape — not *tell me something I don't know* but *say the thing you've been not saying* — and he waited, and the fire settled, and the Bell Quarter was quiet outside, and his father's hands shook on the table.

"I let her take him," his father said. "I could have — there were things I could have done. Things I should have looked at more carefully before I let it go to ratification. I was afraid of what she held over us and I was afraid of what was in the journals and I told myself the twelve years was real and that Perin would be home and everything would—" He stopped. "I chose the bells. I told myself I was protecting the family. I was protecting the bells."

The sentence hung in the kitchen air.

Cass sat with it. He didn't say anything. He looked at his father's face — the specific exhaustion of a man who has just said the true version of something he's been saying the false version of for ten years — and he felt something he didn't have a clean name for. Not forgiveness. Not anger. Something that sat between them like an interval that hadn't resolved yet.

"The contract," he said finally. "The copy you have. The original ratification."

"Yes."

"I want to see it."

His father looked at him.

"I'm not asking to argue," Cass said. "I'm asking because I need to know what it actually says. The key it was ratified in. The terms as written. Whether there's a mechanism for challenging the codicils." He paused. "Perin has been in there ten years. If the codicils are void — if any of them were composed wrong, if there's a lame chord, if the breath limit was exceeded on an amendment — then they don't hold. And if they don't hold, the contract runs ten years, and in two years he comes home."

His father was very still.

"And if the codicils are valid?" he said.

"Then I need to know that too."

His father looked at the table. At the letter. At his hands, which had stopped shaking — the stillness that came over him sometimes, the same stillness as the lathe, a body that had found the one thing it could do when everything else was too much.

He pushed back his chair and stood. He went to the kitchen wall — the one that adjoined the locked room — and Cass watched him press his palm flat against the stone for a moment, the gesture he made when he passed it, brief and almost unconscious. Then he took his hand away and went to the hook by the door where his keys hung.

He took the smallest one off the ring.

He set it on the table in front of Cass.

"The contract is with the journals," he said. "The locked room. Third shelf, the brown folder." He paused. "The bells in there — don't touch them."

Cass looked at the key on the table.

"I'll make supper," his father said. He went to the fire and began building it up, his back to the room, his hands steady now for the first time since he'd walked in from the workshop.

Cass picked up the key. It was small and old, the bow worn smooth from years of handling. He turned it over once.

He got up from the table and went to the locked room.
---

# Chapter Five: Founding Day

The city slept an extra hour on Founding Day.

But the morning Civic Pitch came forty-five minutes after sunrise like any other day, and then the bells went quiet, and the market stalls stayed shuttered, and the song-brokers locked their offices, and even the most punctual of the inner-district merchants found reasons to linger over their bread. The city breathed out. It had been holding its breath for three hundred and sixty-four days, and now it let go.

Cass had loved this since he was small.

He stood at the workshop window while his father finished the last pre-holiday check on the bells — a ritual that took two hours and involved a tuning mallet, a pitch-gauge, and that focused silence his father wore like a second skin in the workshop. Outside, the Bell Quarter was doing its slow morning stretch. A woman from two doors down was hanging washing. A boy Cass didn't recognize was kicking a stone along the center of the lane, which he would never have done on a regular day — the acoustic properties of the Bell Quarter meant sounds off the stone pavement carried a surprising distance, and kicking things was considered a minor social offense. Today, nobody cared. The boy kicked his stone. The woman hung her washing. Somewhere past the rim, someone was singing — not a contract, not a ratification, just a song, rising and falling with the careless pleasure of a voice let off its leash.

Cass pressed his fingers against the cold glass and listened.

No under-note. Not yet. It was there in the background most mornings — faint, persistent — but this morning the Bell Quarter was quiet enough that even the faint version had nothing to attach to. The woman hanging washing wasn't singing. His father wasn't speaking. The boy with the stone was just a boy with a stone.

He could hear his own pulse.

"You'll be ready by the eleventh hour," his father said from behind him. Not a question.

"Yes," Cass said. Then, because it was Founding Day and he was in a reasonable mood: "Do you want me to carry anything? The mallet, or —"

"I'll carry the mallet."

"Right."

His father struck a test note on the Fourteenth — the bell that covered the lower Merchant Tier, the one that needed the most attention in autumn, its bronze contracting in the cold and pulling the pitch fractionally sharp. The note rang clean through the workshop, bounced off the vaulted ceiling, and settled into the floor.

Cass's chest hummed. The frequency lived in his ribs, right of center, as it always did with the Fourteenth. He had mapped the bells in his body years ago without meaning to — the Seventh of Ash Street in his left shoulder, the Twenty-Second in the base of his skull, the Fourteenth in his ribs, right of center. On good days it felt like a hand pressed flat against his chest.

Today was a good day.

"Clean," he said.

His father set down the mallet.

---

The Civic Square filled from the edges inward.

By the eleventh hour it was dense with people, standing in loose groups, the good-natured press of a city that had given itself permission to simply be present somewhere. Children ran between the adults' legs. A vendor near the south entrance was selling hot drinks from a cart — not a licensed stall, no contract required, just a woman with a copper pot and a ladle, and a line of people willing to pay a copper quarter for the warmth of it. Cass could hear the coins ringing as they changed hands. Each one a clean A, as they should. No flats in the square today.

His father stood beside him, hands in his coat pockets. They had walked from the Bell Quarter together without speaking much, which was the normal texture of their walking together. The cold had come in overnight — it sat in the limestone and stayed there — and the square smelled of woodsmoke and that clean sharpness of cold stone in morning light.

Around them, the city talked.

"—said the Forty-Third rang flat last year, but I didn't hear it—"

"You never hear anything, Da."

"I heard *that.*"

A group of Academy students had clustered near the fountain, still in their gray wool even on a holiday, which Cass found faintly depressing. One of them — a third-year he vaguely recognized, tall with a copper-colored scarf that probably wasn't technically a sash but was doing its best to imply one — was holding forth about the original composition.

"Aldric's sequence is a D-major resolution at the end, which is why it feels complete. The major seventh before the final chord is what gives it the emotional weight. It's quite sophisticated for the period."

"It's bells," said the girl next to him. "It's forty-seven bells. It doesn't have emotional weight, it has *mass.*"

Cass almost said something. He stopped himself. He didn't know them and it wasn't worth it and also the girl was at least partly right.

Closer to the tower, two small children were conducting an argument at a volume that suggested they'd been raised in the outer districts. Not a criticism.

"I want to stand at the front."

"You *were* at the front. You moved."

"I didn't *move*, I went to see the vendor and then came back and you were *in my spot.*"

"There's no spots. It's a square."

"There's *my* spot."

Their mother, a broad woman in dark wool with the stained hands of someone who worked near the dye-vats, put one hand on each of their heads without looking away from the Bell Tower. "Both of you," she said, "will be quiet in thirty seconds or we're going home and you can hear the bells from the lane."

Silence, immediate and complete, from both children.

Cass was a little impressed.

"The Seventh's been running sharp," his father said, beside him. Not to anyone in particular. Just noting it.

"Since the last week of summer," Cass said. "I heard it from the kitchen."

His father glanced at him. "You didn't say."

"I thought you'd already caught it."

"I had." A pause. "It'll need attention before the quarter-feast."

"I know." Cass hesitated. "Do you want me to —"

"After the holiday."

Right. After the holiday. He filed it away.

The Bell Tower stood at the square's center: forty feet of dressed limestone, the original stonework Aldric Bellwright had laid two hundred years ago, the bells hanging in their chambers above, invisible from here but present as large things always are, even unseen. Cass had climbed the tower twice, both times with his father, both times for maintenance work. Up close the bells were bronze and metal-dust and the faint char of old flux, and their size made you aware of your own smallness in a way that was not unpleasant.

The crowd quieted.

Not all at once. In waves, from the center outward, as sound moves. Someone near the tower stopped talking, and then the people around them stopped, and the silence spread through the square like a stone dropped in still water. Even the two children from the outer districts went still, the argument about spots apparently suspended by mutual agreement.

His father took his hands out of his pockets.

The first bell rang.

The sequence Aldric had composed ran forty-seven bells over twelve minutes, and Cass had heard it every year of his life, and it was still the thing he could not prepare for.

It started with the deepest bell — the Forty-Seventh, the one that covered the outer districts near the northern rim, the lowest note in the system — a sound that was less heard than felt, a pressure in the sternum that arrived before the ears had processed anything. The Forty-Seventh rang once, twice, three times, and then held its resonance while the crowd stood in it.

Then the Forty-Sixth entered, a minor third above.

Then the Forty-Fifth, a perfect fourth.

The sequence built slowly, each bell entering on its interval, the overtones of the earlier bells still ringing when the new ones joined. This was the craft of it — the bells were tuned not just to their own pitch but to the cumulative resonance of the bells that preceded them, so that each new entry found a harmonic already waiting for it in the air. Aldric had spent eleven years on this. His son had spent his whole life maintaining it.

"There," his father said, very quietly. Not to Cass. To himself, or to no one, or to the bells.

Cass looked at him.

"The Twenty-Ninth," his father said. He was watching the tower with the focused attention he gave the lathe. "Hear how it sits against the Thirtieth."

Cass listened. The Twenty-Ninth had just entered, a major second above the Thirtieth's lingering resonance, and the two together produced a shimmer in the overtone — not wrong, but particular, a texture you could spend years learning to notice.

"It's always done that," Cass said.

"Yes."

"Is it a problem?"

His father was quiet for a moment. The Twenty-Eighth entered above them. "No," he said. "It's what Aldric wanted. The shimmer is intentional. I spent six years thinking it was an error before I understood that."

Cass looked back at the tower. He stored that away — not the fact of it, but the shape of the thing, that his father had spent six years wrong about something and then understood it. That this was a thing that happened.

The air changed. By the time the middle bells were ringing, the sound had a thickness that was different from the sum of its parts, something that pressed gently against the inside of Cass's skull in a way that was not pain.

He had never had the words for this. He had tried, once, in a journal he kept at the back of his wardrobe. *The bells make the air heavier,* he had written, and then crossed it out. *The bells make the air present.* Closer. Still not right.

The twenty-third bell entered. The twenty-second. The twenty-first.

The under-note was still silent.

He knew it before he checked for it — as you know a headache is gone, the absence of a thing that had been so constant you'd stopped noticing it until it wasn't there. Even in the workshop, even on the best days, there was a thread of it somewhere, thin as a wire, running under everything.

Not now.

The fifteenth bell entered. The fourteenth — his ribs rang with it, right of center. The thirteenth.

Cass closed his eyes.

The silence where the under-note lived was not empty. It was full of something else, something the under-note's constant presence had been sitting on top of. Without it, he could hear the thing beneath the sequence more clearly: older and lower than any of the forty-seven bells, a resonance that ran through the ground under his feet and up through the bones of his legs and settled in his chest like a question he didn't have the vocabulary to answer.

 He had not told anyone.

The tenth bell. The ninth. The eighth.

He breathed.

The cold air came into his lungs and the sound came with it, and for twelve minutes on one day a year, Cass Bellwright stood in the Civic Square and was not in pain. The needle behind his left eye was gone, and with it the faint nausea that lived at the back of his throat on bad days. The greasy, curdled wrongness that attached itself to lies and coerced oaths and the small daily violations of a city that had built coercion into its foundations — gone.

The last three bells entered together: a chord that resolved the whole sequence into a major seventh, held, and then released.

Silence.

Real silence, the kind the square hadn't heard since last year.

Cass stood in it with his eyes closed.

He let the seconds pass uncounted. He had counted them when he was younger — had wanted to know exactly how long it lasted, had thought that knowing the number would help him hold onto it. It didn't. The silence lasted as long as it lasted, and then the crowd began to breathe again, and one of the children near the tower said *there,* very softly, to her sister, and the day resumed.

He opened his eyes.

His father was watching him.

Not watching a stranger, not even watching something interesting. The way you watch something you already understand and cannot speak about. His father's hands were in his coat pockets again, but Cass could see the shape of them through the wool — the left one was still, and the right one was moving, the small rhythmic tremor that lived in his father's right hand everywhere except the workshop.

His father looked at him for three seconds.

Cass looked back.

For a moment he thought his father was going to say something. The man's jaw moved, almost — a small shift, the preparation for speech.

Then he looked away, toward the Bell Tower, and the crowd began to thin around them, and the vendor with the copper pot was ladling out drinks again, and Founding Day continued.

"Soup?" his father said.

Cass blinked. "What?"

"It's almost noon."

"Right. Yes. Soup."

They walked, without further discussion, toward the south side where the bread sellers were.

---

They ate at the kitchen table.

His father had cooked the grain soup himself, as he always did on Founding Day: thick with the last of the autumn root vegetables, the recipe written in his mother's hand on a card that lived in the drawer under the bread board. The bread was the outer-district kind, with seeds in the crust, the kind his mother had preferred. His father always bought that kind on Founding Day. Cass had never asked why.

The silence at the main meal was city custom. You didn't talk over your food because a city where words had legal weight needed at least one daily pause from the labor of careful speech. Outsiders found it strange. Cass had grown up in it and found the alternative — the tourist families who ate in the square on Founding Day and talked through the whole meal without seeming to notice — somehow exhausting to watch.

He lasted about four minutes.

"The Academy student near the fountain," he said. "The one with the scarf. He said the sequence ends on a D-major resolution."

His father looked up.

"He's wrong, isn't he. The final chord isn't a resolution. It's a suspension."

His father set his spoon down. He looked at Cass with the expression he used when a student at the workshop said something almost correct. "The seventh resolves to the octave," he said. "That's a resolution."

"But the bass note doesn't move. The Forty-Seventh is still ringing when the final chord lands. So it's not a clean resolution, it's —"

"It's both," his father said. "The upper voices resolve. The bass holds." He picked up his spoon again. "Aldric was doing something specific with that."

"What?"

A pause. His father looked at his soup.

"What was he doing with it?" Cass said.

"Eat your bread."

Cass ate his bread. He ate it with more force than was strictly necessary. The soup was good — it was always good on Founding Day, something about the recipe and the cold and the fact that his father only made it once a year — but the bread required his full attention for a moment.

He tried again. "The thing beneath the sequence," he said, keeping his voice even. "The harmonic. The low one, below the Forty-Seventh's range. Can you —"

"Cass."

"I'm just asking if you can hear it."

His father's right hand was resting on the table beside his bowl. The tremor in it was visible, a small rhythmic motion like something ticking down.

"I hear what the bells are tuned to produce," his father said. "That's all I hear."

Which was not an answer to the question Cass had asked, and they both knew it.

The kitchen smelled of the soup and old wood and, faintly, from the wall to his left, bronze.

The locked room was through that wall.

Cass looked at the wall. He looked at his father. His father was looking at his soup with the focused attention of a man who had decided the soup required it.

"The locked room," Cass said.

The spoon stilled.

"I'm not asking to go in," Cass said, before his father could say whatever he was going to say. "I'm asking what the bells in there are tuned to. Because I can feel them from here and they're tuned to something, and it's the same interval as the harmonic in the Civic Pitch, and I've been hearing that harmonic for six months and I'd like to know what it is."

His father looked at him.

"I know what I heard," Cass said.

The silence that followed was not the comfortable Founding Day silence they'd had during the first four minutes. It was the other kind — the kind with weight and edges. His father's right hand was very still now, which was, Cass had learned, worse than the tremor.

"Eat your dinner," his father said.

"It's lunch."

"Eat it."

Cass picked up his spoon. He ate. His father ate. Outside, the city was celebrating — children in the lane, the distant sound of someone playing a stringed instrument in one of the squares, the general loosened quality of a city that had given itself an afternoon.

His father refilled his bowl from the pot on the stove without asking if he wanted more, which was how the man expressed care, and Cass held the bowl in both hands for the warmth of it and said nothing else.

The under-note was still quiet. He had maybe another hour of this.

He ate his soup and did not look at the wall.

---

The afternoon came in gray and cold, the light going early as it did in the deep part of autumn, the bowl's rim cutting off the low sun before the fourth hour past noon. His father went to the workshop — not to work, just to be there, the place the man went as other people went to gardens or to the window seat. Cass heard the door close. Heard the silence of the workshop when no one was working in it. A different silence from the workshop in use, fuller somehow, the bells on their iron frames holding their potential energy.

He went and sat outside the locked room.

He didn't plan to. He had told himself he was going to the kitchen for water, and then he was standing in the kitchen, and then he was sitting with his back against the wall beside the door, his knees up, his hands in his lap.

The door was old oak, the same age as the house, with a lock that was newer — the lock his father had added the winter after Perin left, a heavy iron thing with no key Cass had ever seen. He had tried the door once, when he was nine. He had not tried it since.

He sat with his back against the wall and listened.

Through the door: nothing. The absence of the ordinary sounds of a room in use — no breath, no movement, no creak of wood settling. Just the bells.

He could feel them. Three of them, hung in the dark on whatever frame his father had built, unrung for as long as Cass had been alive. Their resonance was faint enough that he might have imagined it on any other day, but the under-note was still quiet, and in the quiet he could feel the three bells the way you feel a tooth that's been loosened — not quite pain, a constant awareness of something that was there and shouldn't be touched.

They were tuned to the harmonic.

He had always known this in the abstract — everything in the Bellwright house was tuned to something, that was the nature of the family, the nature of the work. But sitting here with his back against the wall, in the quiet hour of Founding Day, he could feel it with a precision he hadn't had before. Not a note he could name. Not an interval in the Codex. A frequency his body recognized with that recognition of a word you've forgotten sitting just behind your teeth.

The under-note was still silent.

The harmonic from the locked room pressed gently against the wall between them.

Cass sat with it.

The Academy library. The blank pages at the back of Volume Four. The Voss paper that stopped mid-sentence. The interval that didn't exist in the Codex, sitting below F, the one that someone had been asking about in the Bellwright journals. Suvaine's voice, the previous afternoon, moving away from him down the Academy arcade.

Perin's letters. *Tell him the Seventh of Ash Street always ran a quarter-flat in autumn. He'll know what to do.* Perin writing about bells the way a man writes about home when he can't write about home.

He sat there until the cold from the stone floor came through his trousers and into his legs.

Then his father's voice came from the workshop doorway, through the house: "Dinner in half an hour."

Cass stood up. His knees ached. He pressed his once, briefly, then let it go. He looked at the locked door — the old oak, the new iron lock, the dark gap at the threshold where no light came through.

He went to help with dinner.

The under-note came back as he was setting the table, a thin thread of it from somewhere in the street outside — someone ratifying a small transaction at the end of the holiday, the system resuming its business. Faint and familiar.

He set the bowls out. His father came in from the workshop, hands washed, the tremor back in the right one.

"The shimmer in the Twenty-Ninth," Cass said. He was looking at the bowls, not at his father. "You said Aldric wanted it. The specific overtone when it sits against the Thirtieth."

His father stopped in the doorway.

"What does it do?" Cass said. "The shimmer. What's it for."

A long pause. Long enough that Cass looked up.

His father was looking at the wall between the kitchen and the locked room. Not at Cass. His hand rested against the doorframe — not gripping it, just touching it, the way he touched the workshop walls, a press that checked something was still there.

"It keeps the sequence honest," his father said.

Then he came in and sat down, and they ate dinner in the ordinary silence, careful and full — the silence that held things rather than rested from them.

Cass turned the answer over while he ate. *Keeps the sequence honest.* It was the most his father had said about the bells' design in years, and it was also, characteristically, an answer that opened into a larger question his father was not going to address. Keeps it honest how. Against what. Why would a sequence need to be kept honest. What would it mean if it weren't.

He thought about the three bells in the dark behind the wall, and what they were tuned to, and the way his father had looked at him in the square this morning — not like a stranger, not like something interesting, but like something he already understood and could not speak about.

His father knew something.

He had known it for ten years.

He was going to keep not saying it.

Cass put his spoon down. Picked it up again. Finished his dinner.

Outside, the city sang.
---

# Chapter Six: What a Lame Chord Sounds Like

The registry hall smelled of chalk dust and old paper and the damp that seeped from stone walls that never fully dried. The registrar who met him had a copper guild pin on her collar and the focused stillness of someone who had answered the same questions for twenty years.

"Bellwright," she said, writing it down. "First name of the contracting party?"

"Perin. P-E-R-I-N."

"Contracting with?"

"The House of Corda."

She went to the index cabinet, pulled a drawer, ran her finger along a row of cards. Pulled one. Looked at it. Looked at him.

"How old are you?"

"Fourteen."

"And your relation?"

"Brother."

She set the card on the counter between them, face down. "I'm sorry," she said, and she did sound it, a little. "The record is sealed under privacy clause 7-C. Standard for service contracts involving minors at the time of signing."

Perin had been sixteen. A minor, technically. Just barely.

"Can I request a review?"

"You can. You'd need a licensed song-broker to file the petition. The fee is forty-three copper, and the review timeline is six to eight weeks from filing."

Forty-three copper. He had eleven, in the tin box under his bed.

"Thank you," he said.

She nodded and returned the card to the drawer. The small metallic click of it closing was the cleanest sound in the room.

---

He was sitting on the worn lip of the Academy's dry fountain when Lenne found him, twenty minutes later, notebook already open, writing something in the margin with her left hand.

"Registry," she said, without looking up. "Perin's contract?"

"Sealed. Privacy clause. Forty-three copper to file a petition, six to eight weeks."

"Seven-C." She closed the notebook. "My mother filed one of those last year. Took nine weeks."

That was not helpful. He didn't say so.

She looked at him sideways. "Your interval's wrong."

He almost laughed — it came up unexpectedly and he swallowed most of it. "What does that mean."

"It means you're running through options and none of them are working and you look like someone handed you a chord that won't resolve. I've seen you in theory class when the interval's wrong. You make that face."

"I don't make a face."

"You make a face."

Across the courtyard, a second-year student was practicing a ratification progression in the south arcade — a major third, held long and clean, two notes locking together the way two hands lock in a confirmed grip, the sound the Academy used to mean *yes, agreed, I am bound to this*. Cass's ear filed it away as correctly formed and moved on.

"The sealed record blocks the petition," he said. "I need a broker to file, which costs money I don't have. And even if I had it, six weeks before anything comes back. But something's happening with the maintenance clause in Perin's contract — I think the timeline matters. I don't know why. I just think it does."

Lenne was quiet in the way she had of checking her work before showing it.

"Torvald Hess," she said.

"Who?"

"Retired dye-merchant. He runs something in the Weavers' District." A pause. "My mother mentioned him once. She was annoyed about it, which is how I remembered. He keeps tally-songs of contract disputes in the outer districts. Twenty years' worth, maybe more. She said he was thorough. She said it the way she says things she doesn't want to be true."

"Is there any chance he'd have records on Corda?"

"Corda has resonance chambers in the Weavers' District. They've been running contracts over there for years." She picked up her notebook, not to write in it, just to have something in her hands. "If anyone was keeping track of those disputes —"

"He would have been."

"That's what I'm thinking."

He looked at the dry fountain basin. Someone had dropped a copper quarter into it years ago and never retrieved it — he could see it down in the stone, green with age, dull. He wondered if it still rang at A when struck.

"Anything he has isn't legally usable," he said.

"No. But you're not trying to file a court case today. You're trying to understand what you're dealing with. Those are different problems."

That was a cleaner way of putting it than he'd managed on his own. He sat with it for a moment, then stood.

"Do you know where in the Weavers' District?"

"Near the dye-vats on the western end." She paused. "That's all I have."

---

The Weavers' District hit him before he reached it — sulfur and indigo fermentation and something he'd never found a name for. Past the Fade Line the acoustic geometry loosened; a flat note was just a flat note. He asked twice for directions and got pointed to a building with a blue-stained doorframe, the color worked into the grain from decades of dye-stained hands.

The room had been a merchant's back office once. Shelves still held sample cards and empty pigment jars. But the center had been cleared for a long table, and on the table were strips of notched wood arranged in rows, weighted at the corners with smooth river-stones. Dozens, maybe more — each about the length of his forearm, the wood worn pale from handling. The notches along the top edge were cut at different depths and angles, in a system he didn't recognize. Small circles of wax pressed into the lower margin — witness seals, but not the Academy's copper guild seals. Something rougher. He picked one up and ran his thumb along the notches.

The man behind the table was large, going soft with age, with hands stained blue-green so permanently it looked like his natural color. He was cupping his left ear toward Cass in a way that was not a gesture, just how he held his head.

"Close the door," he said.

Cass closed it.

"Bellwright boy." He said it before Cass could speak. "I told you to come find me when you had credentials. You don't have credentials yet." He gestured at the chair across the table. "Sit anyway."

Cass sat. He set the strip down, not wanting to be caught holding it.

"I heard you were asking around," Torvald said.

"I only asked two people."

"Two people here is enough." He picked up one of the strips, turned it in his blue-green hands. "What do you need."

"Records on the House of Corda. Service contracts specifically." Cass paused. "I need to understand the structure of a specific contract. The original record is sealed at the Academy."

"Whose contract."

"My brother's."

Torvald set the strip down. He looked at Cass with the kind of attention that wasn't sympathy and wasn't calculation but something between the two — a man taking an accurate measurement.

"Perin Bellwright," he said. "Signed twelve years ago."

Cass went still. "You know it."

"I know of it. Nobody outside Corda and the Academy registry has the contract itself." He shifted in his chair. "But I have tally-songs on four other Corda service contracts from the same period. And I've been watching the maintenance filing on your family's custodianship." He paused. "It went in three days ago."

"I know. We got the notice."

"Then you know they're moving." He looked at Cass steadily. "Question is what you're planning to do about it."

"I'm trying to understand what I'm dealing with first."

"Good answer." Torvald folded his hands on the table. He looked at the strip he'd set down, then back at Cass. "You were at the Corda compound last autumn — don't look surprised, you were seen — and you stood outside the gate for a while. What did you hear?"

Cass looked at him. Torvald's expression didn't change.

"The bells inside were running flat," Cass said. "Wrong for the district. A quarter-tone, maybe less. High register — the pitch range you'd use for codicil ratification. Amendment confirmation."

"Which direction flat."

"Down. They were pulling down from the ratification note."

Torvald was quiet. He looked at Cass the way Cass's father looked at a bell he was trying to diagnose without striking it — gathering information before committing to an interpretation. "Tell me what else you've heard in any Corda context. Exact pitch. Forget the Academy words for it. Just the sound."

So Cass told him about the courtroom the previous spring — a defendant's oath in G-major, coached and correct on the surface, and the thing underneath that had put a needle behind his left eye for the rest of the afternoon.

He tried to describe it. "Like someone shakes your hand but only squeezes with three fingers. You register the handshake. You register something's wrong. It holds one side without holding the other."

"Yes," Torvald said. He said it quietly, the way you say something you've been trying to find the right words for. "Yes, that's exactly it." He reached across the table and picked up a strip — not one of the ones in front of Cass, one from a separate pile he'd been keeping to his left. He turned it so Cass could see the notch-marks along the edge. "This is what that looks like in a tally-song. The Academy calls it a lame chord. I've been calling it a trap interval for twenty years, but lame chord is better. A binding that grips one party without gripping the other." He set the strip down between them and put his finger on a specific notch. "See this mark? Ratification interval. Major third — your handshake, both parties squeezing. Binding confirmed." He moved his finger to a notch further along, angled differently, cut shallower. "This is the codicil added in year two. See how the direction changes? The Academy notation would call this an ascending resolution. It should go down. It went up." He looked at Cass. "One hand squeezing. The other hand thinking it shook."

Cass looked at the two notches. The difference between them was, to him, invisible — the same depth, the same approximate angle, nothing his eye could catch. But he ran his thumb along them the way he'd done with the first strip, feeling for the difference, and there it was. The second notch was cut fractionally shallower on its right side. A lean. A lean in the wrong direction.

"And the person who signed it," Cass said. "Do they know?"

"That's the question, isn't it." Torvald took the strip back. "See, if you're trained — and I mean properly trained, not the three-week course the Academy sells to traders — you might catch it. But most people aren't, are they? They're nervous, they're being rushed, the Court Singer sounds like he knows what he's about, and you trust the form because what else are you going to trust? It's like buying dyed cloth in bad light — the color looks right, the weave feels right, you take it home and the next morning you see it's not the shade you paid for. But you already cut it." He paused. "That's what a lame chord does. You walk away bound to something you didn't agree to, wearing a color you didn't choose, and you spend years trying to work out why it sits wrong."

He rubbed his stained thumb along the strip's edge. "And that's the thing that makes it complicated — she's not wrong that the outer districts needed someone who understood contracts. Before Corda, people out here got eaten alive by the brokers. Maret built something real. She just built it on a lie."

Cass pressed his tongue to the roof of his mouth. "Come back tomorrow," Torvald said. "I'll have the records pulled. The four Corda service contracts I mentioned, and what I have on the maintenance filings. You can look at them yourself." He paused. "Can you read folk notation?"

"No."

"I'll teach you enough to follow along. You read Academy notation?"

"Yes."

"Same intervals. Different marks." He went back to sorting the tally-songs. "Go home. Get some sleep."

Cass stood. He reached for the chair back, steadied himself. "Hess."

Torvald looked up.

"The maintenance filing. The access clause. Do your records show whether Corda has used it before? The acoustician access to the bells?"

Torvald looked at him for a moment with that same measuring attention. "That's exactly the right question," he said. "Come back tomorrow and I'll tell you what I found."

He walked Cass toward the district's edge.

The Fade Line wasn't marked. You knew it because you felt the air change — sound stopped being gathered by the limestone curve and became ordinary, undirected. Cass felt it as a loosening behind his ears.

A table was set up at the intersection where cobblestones gave way to packed earth. A song-broker — not Academy-licensed; cotton sash, not copper-threaded — sat behind it with contract parchment and a tuning fork. Across from him: a family. A man with his hands flat on the table. A woman holding the younger child's wrist the way you hold something you're afraid will be taken.

The broker struck the fork and began to sing.

Cass heard the form immediately — the ratification interval, the major third, the handshake. The pitch was correct. The vowels sat in the standard positions for service-contract ratification: *yes, agreed, I accept these terms.*

The man nodded along as though he understood the structure. He didn't. Cass could see it in his eyes — tracking the broker's mouth instead of his hands, following the performance rather than the content. The woman wasn't looking at the broker at all. She was looking at the children.

Then the under-note.

It came in on the codicil — the amendment clause — threaded into the overtone the way a wire threads through wool. The surface note was clean. Underneath, the interval resolved upward. Wrong direction. The ascending resolution Torvald had shown him on the tally-strip twenty minutes ago. One hand squeezing while the other thought it shook.

The asymmetric chord gripped the family — all of them, the man and the woman and the children's futures — while the other party remained free. A lame chord, composed in real time, at a table on the Fade Line, for people who didn't know what they were hearing.

Cass started forward. Torvald's hand closed on his arm.

"You don't have credentials," Torvald said quietly. "You interrupt a binding in progress without standing and they file against your whole family. Corda has another name on a contract before sundown."

Cass stood still. The broker sang on. The man reached for the pen because the form was correct and the sound was correct and what else was he going to trust. The woman's hand tightened on the child's wrist.

Torvald watched it happen with the expression of a man who had seen this many times and would see it again — not anger, not resignation, but the thing between them that had no name because no one who felt it had ever been in a position to stop it.

"That's what it looks like," he said. "Every day."

They watched the family sign. The broker's fork rang once more — bright, completed — and the family took their copy and stood. The man shook the broker's hand. He was smiling. The woman was not.

Cass turned away. The under-note sat in his jaw like a dull ache that would stay for hours.

"Go home," Torvald said again. "Get some sleep."

---

He came back the next afternoon. Lenne was already there.

She was sitting at the near end of the table with her notebook open, and she looked up when Cass came in with an expression that was not quite apologetic. "I thought another set of eyes wouldn't hurt," she said.

Torvald, at the far end, didn't look up from the strip he was reading. "She reads folk notation," he said. "Badly, but she reads it. Sit down."

Cass sat. He looked at Lenne. She looked back.

"Your mother know you're here?" he said.

"No." She said it without hesitation, which told him she'd thought about it and decided anyway. He let it go.

The table was covered. More strips than yesterday, weighted with ledger books. The room held sound dead and close.

Torvald had already taught Cass the basics of the notation — the deep notch for ratification, the lean of the codicils indicating direction. Cass could feel the difference now: lean left for a correct resolution, lean right for a lame chord. Lenne couldn't yet, which bothered her. She'd get there.

Torvald moved to the far end of the table and began laying strips into groups. "Corda service contracts. I have four from the decade surrounding your brother's signing. Not the contracts themselves — the tally-songs from disputes that arose under those contracts. Wage disputes, mostly. One boundary complaint." He tapped a group of three strips. "This group here is interesting. The contracting party filed a codicil amendment in the second year, tried to limit their service hours. The amendment was rejected by the Corda-retained Court Singer on the grounds of improper interval." He paused. "It wasn't improper. I had three witnesses and a licensed broker from the Potters' Quarter look at the tally-song at the time. The interval was clean. The Court Singer said it wasn't."

"What happened to the contracting party?" Lenne said.

"Still in service. Ninth year now." He moved to the next group. "This one resolved. The party paid out the penalty clause and left. Took them four years to save enough." He set that group aside. "And this one —"

He stopped.

Cass looked at him. Something had changed in his face — the patient precision gone. Something harder and faster, the expression of a man who has opened a drawer and found it empty when he knows he filled it.

"What is it," Cass said.

Torvald set down the strip he was holding. He moved along the table to the far end, where the shelves were, and ran his blue-green finger along a row of notches in the shelf-edge itself — a catalogue system, Cass realized, the same notch-marks as the strips, the shelf recording what should be where. Torvald ran his finger back along it. Stopped. Ran it forward again.

"I have four Corda service contracts from that decade," he said. "Or I should." He turned around. His voice was level and his face was not. "The fourth one is gone. The strip has been pulled."

The room went quiet in a way that had nothing to do with acoustics.

"Which one," Cass said.

"The one from twelve years ago." Torvald looked at him steadily. "The one that would cover your brother's signing year."

Nobody spoke. Lenne had stopped writing. Her pen was resting against the page and her hand wasn't moving.

"Could it have been misfiled," she said.

"No." He said it without looking at her, still looking at Cass. "I know this archive. I built it. Every strip has a catalogue mark and a shelf position and I check them twice a year." He moved back to the table and sat down heavily, the chair creaking under him. "That one was there six months ago. I checked it myself."

Torvald put both hands flat on the table. Then he stood again — fast, the chair scraping stone — and went back to the shelf and ran his finger along the catalogue notches a third time, his nail catching on the wood.

"Twenty years," he said. The patient measuring tone was gone. What replaced it was the voice of a craftsman looking at damage that should never have been possible. "Twenty years of records. Every dispute, every bad codicil, every contract in this district that didn't sound right. I cut every one of those strips myself." He turned around. His face was dark. "And someone walked into my workshop and put their hands in my shelves and pulled the *one strip* that would have shown what Corda did to your brother."

He picked up the nearest river-stone and set it down hard enough to shake the table. The remaining strips shifted in their rows.

"This is my house. These are my records. Whoever did this stood where you're standing and chose which piece of twenty years' work to destroy." His hand was trembling. He pressed it flat to still it. "I want you to understand what this is. This isn't a misfiled document. This is someone reaching into my life and telling me they can take whatever they want, whenever they want, and I'll never know when it happened."

Lenne's pen was motionless on the page.

"Someone came in here," Cass said.

"And they knew what to look for." Torvald said it quiet and exact. "You don't pull one strip from forty years of records by accident. You pull it because you know it's there and you know what it shows."

Lenne looked up from her notebook. "Who has access to this room?"

"People I trust." He paused. "People I trusted." He picked up one of the remaining strips and set it down again. "The Compact has twelve members. Three of them have been here in the last six months on business. I'm not going to tell you it wasn't one of them, because I don't know that yet."

"Or someone else," Cass said. "Someone who knew the Compact existed and knew what you kept here and had a reason to be watching."

Torvald looked at him. "Yes," he said. "Or that."

Cass felt the shape of his plan — the path from Torvald's records to the Civic Court to a petition that could challenge Perin's contract — collapsing inward. Without the fourth strip, he had three contracts showing matching malformations and a gap exactly where the proof should be. He could demonstrate a pattern. He could not show the pattern touched Perin. Everything he'd built toward for two days led to a door that was no longer there.

"This changes things," he said. "I can't go to the Civic Court with three strips and an empty shelf."

"No," Torvald said. The anger was still in his hands, in the set of his jaw, but he was watching Cass with that testing attention — waiting to see whether the boy would fold or turn.

"Whoever took the strip knows I'm looking, and they're ahead of me," Cass said. "If I keep following the paper trail, they'll keep clearing it." The thought was forming as he spoke, and he let it form. "I have to come at the sound itself. Not the record of what was sung — the actual interval. If the lame chord is in Perin's contract, it's still there. You can burn a strip. You can't unring a bell."

Torvald went still. The anger didn't leave — it was in every line of him — but something else moved behind it. Recognition, maybe.

"That's a different kind of path," he said.

"It's the only one I've got."

"The three contracts you still have," Cass said. "Is the pattern in all of them?"

Torvald laid the remaining strips out in order. His finger moved along the notch-marks, and Cass watched his face. In each contract, the same thing: ratification clean, year-two codicil leaning wrong. The lame chord, three times running.

"Same Court Singer on all three." Torvald turned one of the strips so Lenne could see the witness marks. "Corda-retained. Married to a Corda cousin."

Three contracts with the same malformation in the same position. And a fourth — Perin's signing year — gone.

"If the fourth one showed the same pattern," he said.

"Then it's not an accident," Torvald said. "And someone knows it's not an accident. And they'd rather you didn't know it either."

Lenne put her pen down. She'd been writing steadily since Torvald started laying out the strips, filling her notebook page in the small fast hand she used when she was trying to get everything down before she'd decided what mattered. Now she stopped.

"Okay," she said. "So what do we do."

"We don't do anything yet," Cass said. "We don't have the fourth strip."

"We have three contracts showing the same illegal codicil construction."

"They're not legally usable. Torvald said so yesterday."

"I said they weren't usable in a court case," Torvald said. He was watching them both. "That's not the same as useless."

"It's the same as not enough," Cass said. "If we go to a Court Singer with three folk-notation tally-songs and no fourth strip and no access to the sealed registry record —"

"I'm not saying we go to a Court Singer today," Lenne said. "I'm saying we know something now that we didn't know yesterday. That's not nothing."

"It's also not Perin."

She looked at him. "I know it's not Perin."

"Then what are you suggesting we do with it? Go to the Academy? File a complaint? With what? Forty-three copper we don't have and a six-to-eight-week timeline while Corda's maintenance filing goes through and they get access to the bells?"

"I'm suggesting we don't panic."

"I'm not panicking."

"You're getting loud," Torvald said mildly.

Cass closed his mouth. He was, in fact, getting loud.

"I'm sorry," he said. To Lenne, not to the room.

"You're catastrophizing," she said. "That's different from thinking."

He looked at her. She looked back, steady and precise and annoyingly correct.

"Fine," he said. "What are you thinking?"

She picked up her pen and turned to the next blank page in her notebook. "I'm thinking there's a pattern across three contracts. Same Court Singer, same malformation, same year-two timing. That's not an accident. That's a method." She wrote something, showed it to him — not words, intervals, the Academy notation she'd been trained in, set next to the folk notation marks she'd been copying from the strips. "And if it's a method, it's been used before these four contracts and it'll be used again. Which means there are other records. Not here — Torvald's already checked, and whoever pulled the fourth strip would have looked at the others too. But the disputes that arose under those contracts — some of them would have gone to the Civic Court. Public record. Not sealed."

Cass looked at the notation on her page. "The Civic Court records are indexed by contracting party."

"Yes."

"We don't have names for the contracting parties in the three strips. The tally-songs use a witness code, not a name."

"Two of them resolved," Torvald said. "I can give you names for those. The third — the one still in service — I'll need to think about whether that's mine to share."

"I understand," Lenne said, and she said it with the particular formality she used when she was taking something seriously and wanted you to know it.

"The fourth strip," he said. "The one that's missing. When you checked it six months ago — do you remember what it showed?"

Torvald was quiet for a moment. Outside, the work song continued, unhurried.

"I remember," he said, "that I thought I should look at it more carefully. I remember thinking I'd come back to it." He set the strip down. "I didn't come back to it."

The wrongness of it settled in his chest and stayed. Someone was ahead of him, cleaning the shelves before he arrived.

"Hess," he said.

Torvald looked up.

"Is there anywhere else? Any other archive that might have covered the same contracts? Anyone in the Compact who was keeping their own records at the time?"

"One person," Torvald said. He said it slowly, like he was checking the weight of it before he put it down. "She moved to the Flood Mouth eight years ago. I haven't spoken to her since." He looked at the empty space on the shelf where the fourth strip should have been. "I'll write to her." He paused. "There's also a Court Singer — independent, not Corda-retained. Name's Sorn. He's heard things too, but he won't move without evidence."

"How long will that take?"

"Two weeks if she's prompt. Three if she's not."

Three weeks. The maintenance filing was already in. Three weeks was a long time to wait while Corda moved toward the bells.

"How long to learn to read these properly?" Cass said.

"Enough to follow along? An hour." Torvald looked at him. "Enough to argue it before a court? Years."

"I don't need a court today."

Lenne moved her chair around to Cass's side of the table without being asked, notebook open.

"Hess," Lenne said. She was looking at her notebook, not at either of them. "The lame chord in these contracts — does it hold the contracting party or the Corda party?"

Torvald looked at her. Something shifted in his expression, quick and considered. "That," he said, "is a very good question."

"Because if it holds the contracting party —"

"Then the Corda party isn't bound at all," Cass said. He heard his own voice come out flat and certain, the way it came out when he'd heard something he couldn't unhear. "The contract looks bilateral. It reads bilateral. The Court Singer confirms it as bilateral. But one hand is squeezing and the other hand is free." He set the strip down. "Perin is bound. Corda isn't."

The room was quiet.

"That's —" Lenne stopped.

"Yes," Torvald said.

"That's the whole thing," she said. "That's why it doesn't expire. A real contract has release terms that bind both parties. But if Corda isn't actually bound —"

"There's nothing to release," Cass said. "Because there's nothing holding them. The release would only apply to Perin, and the penalty clause for unilateral release —" He stopped. He already knew the answer. He'd known it, he realized, since his father had said *your brother is well, he writes*, in that specific flat voice. He'd just needed the vocabulary to name it. "The penalty clause for unilateral release absorbs the breaching party. Which is the only party who's actually bound."

Lenne was looking at him.

"So he can't leave," she said.

"He can't leave without triggering a penalty that finishes him. And Corda can walk away any time they like because the lame chord never gripped them." He exhaled. "Twelve years."

"Cass —"

"Twelve years and the whole time the contract's been —" He stopped himself. His jaw was tight. "Sorry. I know. We need to think."

"You're allowed to be angry," she said.

"I know I'm allowed." He looked at the strips on the table. At the absent space on the shelf. "Being angry doesn't get Perin out."

"No," Torvald said. "It doesn't." He looked at Cass steadily. "But understanding what you're dealing with might. And now you understand it."

Outside, the work song went on — minor key and untroubled. Cass looked at the three tally-songs laid out across the table: notched wood and wax seals, because the Academy's records cost forty-three copper and six to eight weeks.

"Teach me the rest of it," he said.

Torvald picked up a strip. Lenne's pen moved. And Cass bent over the table and began to learn a different way of reading what the intervals said.
---

# Chapter Seven: The Bellwright-Corda Agreement

His father was at the lathe when Cass came in.

The workshop had gone to its night-self: lamplight making the hanging bells into shadows, linseed oil heavier without the forge's competing heat. Eddan worked with his back to the door, hands moving along a clapper shaft, testing the curve. He didn't turn when Cass set his bag on the stone bench.

Cass came further in and stopped at the edge of the lamp's reach.

"I need to ask you something."

The lathe kept turning.

"Mm."

"The Bellwright-Corda agreement. The one from twelve years ago."

His father's hands went still. The lathe slowed. Stopped.

Eddan set the clapper down with the care of a man who needed something to do with his hands, and turned.

"What about it," he said.

"I want to know what it covers."

"The maintenance arrangement. Quarterly reports. Access to the Corda resonance chambers for tuning." His father's voice was flat. "You've seen the notices."

"I've seen the notices. I haven't seen the agreement."

"It's a standard — it's..." He stopped. "Standard custodianship. The Corda family holds a — the bronze stock." A pause. "We maintain. We file reports."

"What kind of claim?"

"An old one." His father folded the cloth he'd been using. Set it down. "Aldric's son signed a document. Before the tonal system. Written, not sung. The Corda family holds it."

"Is it enforceable?"

Something crossed his father's face, quick and gone. "It's a historical document. Under current Tonal Law—"

"Is it enforceable."

The cooling lathe ticked once.

"No," his father said. "It isn't."

"But she used it anyway."

His father looked at him. The lamp threw shadows up under his eyes. "She raised the question," he said. "Twelve years ago. Whether the Academy should examine the claim. Whether the Bellwright family's custodianship was—" He stopped. Started again. "The word she used was *fitness*."

Cass's chest went tight.

"Fitness," he said.

"Whether the Bellwright family was fit to maintain sole custodianship of the forty-seven bells. It would have required a formal review. The Academy examining the journals. The bronze stock records. The casting methods." He paused. "Everything."

"And Perin signed the service contract instead."

His father said nothing.

"That's what it cost," Cass said. "To make her not raise the question."

Still nothing. His father had turned slightly, not away but angled, as if the bells on the wall needed looking at.

Cass watched the back of his head for a moment. Then he said, "I'm going to the public registry. Tonight. The original filing will be there."

"Cass—"

"I'm not asking for your copy." He picked up his bag. "I wanted you to know I was going."

He walked out before his father could find the words, if he had any.

---

The Academy's public registry occupied the ground floor of the oldest building in the district, tight-corridored stone that smelled of chalk and old paper. The registry was open until the evening bell, which gave him forty minutes.

The registrar was a thin man named Adwick who wore copper-colored sleeve guards over his coat. He looked at Cass the way he looked at all Academy students who arrived without a faculty sponsor — measuring.

"Bellwright-Corda agreement," Cass said. "Twelve years ago. Custodianship filing."

Adwick didn't write anything down. He looked at Cass. His eyes went to the shelves behind him, then back, and something in the look was off — a fraction too long, a fraction too careful.

"That's a sealed registry," he said. "Privacy clause, standard for—"

"Custodianship agreements are public record. The bells are a civic instrument."

"The privacy clause covers the codicil amendments. The primary filing is public." Adwick paused. "One moment."

He disappeared into the shelves.
