Cass stood at the counter and pressed his Down the corridor, a student was running scales — descending fifth, over and over, not quite landing the resolution. He made himself stop listening.

Adwick came back with a bound folio, gray-covered, tied with the red cord that marked civic filings. He set it on the counter. Then he set it down again a second time, slightly differently, and said, "You can read it here. It doesn't leave the room," in the tone of a man reciting something he'd been told to say.

"I know," Cass said.

He untied the cord and opened it. Twelve pages, formal Academy script, dense with quarterly maintenance schedules and bronze stock inventories. He read every clause.

He found it on page seven.

He read it once. Read it again.

*Co-Custodial Review Clause. In the event that the House of Corda determines, in its sole assessment, that the Bellwright family's continued custodianship is inconsistent with the standards of fitness required by the Academy's Charter of Civic Instruments, the House of Corda reserves the right to petition the Academy for a formal fitness review. Such a petition will require the Bellwright family to submit all records, journals, and documentation pertaining to the original casting and tuning of the forty-seven bells for examination.*

And below it:

*This co-custodial review right persists for the duration of the Bellwright-Corda service agreement and all amendments thereto, and shall not expire so long as any active service obligation exists between the two parties.*

He looked up.

Adwick was watching him from the far end of the counter. Not working. Watching.

Cass looked back at the page. *So long as any active service obligation exists.* Perin's contract was a service obligation. While Perin was in service, Maret Corda could invoke the fitness review at any moment, for any reason, and the journals opened.

He turned back to page one and looked for the term date.

There wasn't one. The service obligation was referenced by a codicil number. Sealed. He couldn't see when it ended, or whether it ended, or what conditions would satisfy it.

He set his finger on the co-custodial clause and read it a third time.

*Sole assessment.* She didn't need a lame chord. She didn't need a provable breach. She needed to say one word to the Academy and the journals opened. And she could say it any time she chose, for as long as Perin's contract ran.

He closed the folio. Retied the cord.

"Thank you," he said.

Adwick said, "The codicil records. If you wanted to petition for access—"

"I know the process."

Adwick looked at him. "Someone was in yesterday," he said. "Asking about the same filing."

Cass went still.

"Who."

Adwick adjusted his sleeve guard. "I can't say who. I can tell you they weren't a student."

The under-note stirred — not the clean needle-sharp signal of a lie, but something muddier, less certain. A flicker. He'd felt it before in market disputes, when a seller quoted a price they'd already half-convinced themselves was fair. Adwick wasn't lying about someone having been here. That part sat clean in the air between them. But the way he said *I can't say* — not *I don't know*, not *I won't say*, but *I can't* — that flickered. The under-note pulsed once behind Cass's left eye, dimmed, pulsed again. As if Adwick had told himself a story about why he couldn't say, and believed the story just enough for the note to lose its edge.

The man across the counter wasn't lying — or he was, but only about which part of the truth was his to give. The important thing rang clean: someone had been here, someone who wasn't a student, someone who knew what to look for. The evasion wrapped around it was something Adwick had convinced himself was duty rather than cowardice, and the conviction was just strong enough to blur the signal.

Cass kept his voice even. "Did they take anything?"

A pause. Long enough to be an answer. "The filing is complete," Adwick said. "The primary filing."

"The codicil index," Cass said. "Is it complete?"

Adwick's eyes went to the shelves behind him. Came back. He said, "The codicil index is a separate record," in a voice that had gone careful and flat.

"Is it complete."

Adwick said nothing.

He picked up his bag and walked out.

The evening bell rang as he came through the Academy district gate. B-flat, clean and civic, rolling down from the tower. He stood on the road and let it pass and did not feel the usual thing he felt when the bell sounded right.

Someone had been there yesterday. Before him. Not a student — someone who knew the filing well enough to go straight to the codicil index, and who had enough standing in the registry for Adwick to go careful and flat when asked about it. The tally-song strip gone from Torvald's shelf. Now this. Someone moving ahead of him, taking things off shelves before he arrived.

*Sole assessment.* The clause sat in his chest where a clean chord should have been.

He turned onto the road toward the Merchant Tier.

He didn't make a decision about it. He was already walking.

---

The Corda compound sat in the inner ring of the bowl, south slope, where the Merchant Tier kept its walled estates and its cedar-scented money. The Corda compound's gate porter had hands that didn't match his position — broad, roughened, the hands of someone who had worked with materials heavier than paper. Cass filed that away without knowing what to do with it. Cass had stood outside the gate before — once, two years ago, on an errand for his father that had required him to deliver a maintenance report to the porter's window. He'd handed over the folio and walked away fast and not looked back. That time he'd had permission to be there. This time he had the co-custodial clause in his bag and his brother's name in his mouth and absolutely nothing else.

He felt the compound's acoustics before he saw the gate.

It started at the corner of Mercer Lane — a pressure change, subtle, the way your ears register a hillside before your legs do. The Merchant Tier sat close enough to the bowl's center that the amphitheater's geometry was still working, but the Corda compound had its own resonance chambers built into the east wing, and those chambers pushed back against the natural acoustic. Standing at the corner, Cass could feel the interference in his back teeth. A chord that almost resolved, held just short of resolution. The tension of it pressed against the inside of his skull like a thumb against a bruise.

He stood there for a moment. Let his jaw loosen. The interference didn't stop, but he stopped fighting it.

The gate was iron, tall, the kind designed to look permanent. A porter's window sat to the right. A boy about Cass's age was leaning against the wall beside it, eating something wrapped in paper, not paying attention to anything.

Cass walked up to the window.

The porter was a broad man with a gray coat and the specific boredom of someone who had been sitting in a small room for a long time. He looked at Cass.

"Bellwright," Cass said. "I'm here about the maintenance schedule."

The porter looked at him. "You're the Bellwright boy."

"My father sent me." The words came out steady, which surprised him. "There's a discrepancy in the quarterly report. The resonance chamber in the east wing — the tuning record doesn't match the inspection notes from last month. He needs to see the chamber logs before he files the correction."

A pause.

"He usually comes himself."

"His hands," Cass said. "He's been having trouble with the fine work. I've been running the inspections this quarter."

Another pause. The porter looked at him with the expression of a man doing arithmetic he didn't particularly want to do. Cass kept his face neutral and thought about nothing except the specific weight of a pitch-gauge in his hands, the particular angle of a bell inspection, things he actually knew.

"Chamber logs are with the house steward," the porter said. "East corridor, second door."

The gate opened.

Cass walked through.

The compound's interior hit him like walking into a different room inside his own head. The interference was worse here — not louder, but denser, layered in a way that suggested the resonance chambers had been running exercises long enough to leave a residue in the air. He could feel the standing waves from two or three different sessions overlapping, none of them fully resolved, all of them pressing. His left hand went flat against his thigh. He kept walking.

The courtyard was stone-flagged and quiet, the afternoon light cutting across it at a low angle. A woman in a dark coat crossed the far end without looking at him. Somewhere in the east wing, someone was running a minor seventh, over and over, the interval climbing and not landing.

He knew that exercise.

He stopped walking.

The minor seventh, ascending, no resolution — Perin had drilled it for weeks the summer Cass was six, complaining that it was the most useless interval in the codex because it wanted to resolve so badly and the exercise refused to let it. He'd practiced it in the workshop while their father cast, the reaching note hanging in the air, unfinished, until Cass had started hearing it in his sleep.

Someone in the east wing was running Perin's exercise.

He turned toward the sound.

The east corridor was dim, stone-floored, smelling of the cedar wax used on instrument cases and something underneath it, older — the particular dry smell of a space where sound had been made and held for a long time. The minor seventh came clearer here, closer. Second door, the porter had said. Cass passed the first door without stopping.

He stopped at the second.

Knocked.

The minor seventh cut off.

A pause that had a specific quality — not the pause of someone who hadn't heard, but the pause of someone deciding.

"Come in," said a voice Cass had not heard in four years, and the under-note was completely, perfectly silent.

---

Perin was sitting at a writing desk near the window, a manuscript open in front of him, a pen in his hand. He looked up when Cass came in and his face did several things in quick succession that he then put away, one by one, until what remained was something careful and still.

He was twenty-six. He looked older. He'd grown a beard, short and neat, the kind you grew when you'd stopped thinking about whether you had one. His hands on the desk were the same hands Cass remembered — long-fingered, ink-stained, calloused at the same points from the same work. The room around him was orderly in the way of someone who had learned to keep things orderly because it was the one thing they could control: manuscript stacks squared, pen case closed, a cup of something cold on the corner of the desk.

"How did you get in," Perin said.

"Maintenance schedule discrepancy."

His brother looked at him for a long moment. "That's father's line."

"I know."

"He actually has discrepancies. You just used it."

"Yes."

Perin set the pen down. He didn't stand up. He looked at Cass with an expression that was almost familiar — the dry, sideways look he'd had when Cass was small and had done something inadvisable and Perin was deciding whether to be impressed or alarmed. "You're going to get thrown out when they check."

"Probably." Cass came further into the room. "I need to talk to you."

"I can see that."

"The co-custodial clause."

Something shifted in Perin's face. Not surprise — something that had been waiting. He said, "Sit down, Cass," in a voice that had gone quiet.

Cass sat in the chair across from the desk. The window behind Perin showed the compound's inner courtyard, a fountain that wasn't running, the east wing's stone face. The minor seventh was gone from the air. In its place, the interference from the resonance chambers pressed on steadily, that unresolved chord sitting in Cass's back teeth like a sliver of metal.

"How long have you known," Cass said.

"About the clause? Since I signed." Perin's hands were flat on the desk. "I read the whole contract before I ratified. I knew what it was."

"You knew it had no term."

"The codicil was sealed. I didn't know the exact language. But I knew it wasn't—" He stopped. Looked at the window. "I knew it wasn't what father told me it was."

"What did he tell you?"

"That it was temporary. That the Corda family needed a composer for a specific project, two or three years, and that the fitness question would go away when the project was complete." He said it without inflection. Twelve years of saying it to himself. "I was sixteen. I wanted to believe him."

Cass looked at his brother's hands on the desk. At the ink stains at the base of his first two fingers.

"You've been composing their contracts," he said.

"Yes."

"For twelve years."

"Yes."

"And she keeps extending."

Perin didn't answer. That was its own answer.

The interference from the resonance chambers pressed on. Cass's jaw ached with it. He said, "I went to Torvald Hess. The Compact. I've been learning the tally-song system — the way they document lame chord disputes in the outer districts. Arveth Corda-Laine sang your ratification."

Perin went still.

"She's on three of Torvald's disputed agreements," Cass said. "Same structure each time. Minor seventh amendment, no tonic resolution. The codicil floats loose from the original binding because the interval never closed. Torvald's cases are small — two of them settled, one is still pending. But the pattern is the same." He heard himself speaking faster and made himself slow down. "If your standing-wave record shows the same amendment frequency—"

"Cass."

"—that's demonstrable grounds. The Academy keeps acoustic records separate from the contract terms, they don't fall under the privacy clause, and if Arveth Corda-Laine left the amendment interval unresolved across multiple—"

"Cass." Perin's voice was quiet. Not sharp, not tired. Just quiet, in a way that stopped him. "The standing-wave record from my ratification is gone. I looked for it three years ago. Someone filed a correction notice — clerical error, standard form — and the original record was pulled."

Cass felt something drop out of his chest.

"Adwick," he said. "The registrar. Someone was there yesterday, before me. Not a student."

Perin looked at him. "How do you know that?"

"He told me. He was—" He stopped. "He was trying to warn me, I think. He couldn't say it directly. But he told me the codicil index was touched."

Perin was quiet for a moment. "They've been thorough," he said. "They've had twelve years to be thorough."

"So we find another angle." Cass heard the edge in his own voice and didn't try to smooth it. "The co-custodial clause itself — the fact that it's structured so the fitness review can never expire as long as you're in service. That's not a standard clause. Torvald knows a Court Singer, Dav Sorn, who's independent of Corda. If Sorn would examine the original agreement—"

"And say what? That the clause is aggressive?" Perin's voice was still quiet. That was almost worse than if he'd sounded angry. "Aggressive isn't void, Cass. The Academy will look at the interval, confirm the ratification was in proper form, and tell you the terms are the terms."

"The ratification had a lame chord."

"That you can't prove."

"The codicil index was pulled. That's evidence of—"

"That's evidence someone filed a correction notice." Perin picked up the pen and set it down again. The same gesture their father made with the folding cloth. "I've been through this. I've been through every version of this for twelve years."

The room was very quiet. Outside, the fountain didn't run. The interference from the resonance chambers sat in Cass's teeth.

"Then show me what you have," Cass said.

Perin looked at him.

"You've been through every version for twelve years," Cass said. "Show me what you found. Show me what's left."

A long pause. Perin's eyes went to the manuscript on the desk, and then to Cass, and then to something past Cass's shoulder that wasn't in the room. Then he opened the desk drawer and took out a bundle of letters, tied with a cord, and set them on the desk between them.

Cass recognized the handwriting on the top one. His father's.

"He writes every month," Perin said. "Has since the first year."

"He told me you write. He said—" Cass stopped. *Your brother is well, he writes.* "He never said he wrote to you."

"No. He wouldn't." Perin untied the cord. The letters fanned slightly, a thick stack, twelve years of monthly correspondence. "Read the third one from the top."

Cass picked it up. Opened it.

His father's handwriting, cramped and careful. A date four months after Perin's signing. Three paragraphs about the workshop — a new clapper cast for the Seventh of Ash Street, a problem with the bronze stock, the quarterly report filed on time. Then, at the bottom, in smaller script, as if added after:

*I have read the journals. All of them. I want you to know that what I did, I did knowing what was in them, and I did it anyway, and I have not found a way to say that to you that doesn't sound like an excuse. It isn't an excuse. I am telling you so that you know I understood the cost.*

Cass read it twice. Set it down.

"He knows what's in the journals," he said.

"Yes."

"What's in them."

Perin looked at him for a moment. "Something Aldric put into the first bells. Something in the sequence — the Civic Pitch, the cumulative harmonic. It's not in any codex." He paused. "Have you been hearing something in the morning pitch? Something low. Underneath."

The question arrived like a bell struck in a quiet room.

Cass said, "Yes."

Perin nodded slowly, as if something had been confirmed that he'd been carrying for a long time. "I thought you might. You always heard more than you should." He looked at the letters. "It's not a binding. It's not a command. Aldric composed it as — Cass, I've been trying to find the right word for four years. The closest I have is *evidence*. He composed evidence into the bells. Of what the system was built to do and what it was built to hide."

His jaw was tight. He hadn't noticed when it had started.

"The fitness review," he said. "The journals. That's what Maret Corda is actually holding. Not just the bronze stock claim. She knows what's in them."

"She's known for thirty years, from what I can piece together. She built the whole Corda empire partly around the fact that the Bellwright family would do anything to keep those journals closed."

"Including give her you."

Perin said nothing. He reached out and retied the cord around the letters, neat and precise.

"Read the intervals," he said.

"What?"

"In the letters. The technical observations — the chamber tunings, the maintenance notes, the things I describe. I've been writing to you since you were old enough to read them. Not the letters you've seen." He looked at Cass. "The ones I've been putting inside the letters you've seen."

Cass stared at him.

"The specific intervals I mention. The exact frequencies. The sequence they appear in." Perin's voice was still quiet, but something had shifted underneath it — not hope exactly, but the thing that lives in the same house as hope. "I've been building a record. Every lame chord I've composed for them, every amendment that didn't resolve, every codicil that floated loose — I've been encoding the pattern in the maintenance notes. The observations are real. The sequence they appear in is a message."

"To me."

"I didn't know when you'd be old enough to read it. I didn't know if you'd hear what I put in it." He looked at Cass steadily. "But you went to Torvald Hess. You learned the tally-song system. You found Arveth Corda-Laine across three disputed agreements." He paused. "Read them again."

Cass looked at the bundle of letters on the desk between them.

Twelve years of monthly correspondence. His father's cramped handwriting, the small script at the bottom of the third one from the top. *I have read the journals. I want you to know I understood the cost.*

And inside those, something else. A record Perin had been building in the gaps, in the technical observations, in the specific intervals he described — a message written in the only language Maret Corda's people wouldn't think to read, because it was too small and too patient and it had been waiting for someone who could hear what the intervals said.

Outside, the fountain didn't run. The resonance chambers pressed on with their unresolved chord, reaching and reaching and not landing.

Cass picked up the bundle.

"Read them again," Perin said.
---

# Chapter Eight: Dav Sorn

He started with the oldest letter and Lenne sat across from him on the floor, her back against the bed frame, her notation paper on her knees.

They had been at it for two hours. The lamp was burning low and the letters were arranged between them in rows — oldest near the door, newest near Cass's feet. Forty-three letters. Not all of them had intervals. More than expected did.

"This one," Lenne said. She held up a letter from year three. Her index finger was ink-stained from copying out intervals as Cass read them aloud. "He says the lower register wants to be *closer to a minor sixth.* Not a minor sixth. Closer to one."

"Quarter-tone flat," Cass said. He wrote it down.

"That's a technical observation if you're not looking for a message."

"That's the point."

She set the letter in the done pile and picked up the next one. Her lips moved slightly as she read — working through it internally, not translating, just understanding. He'd noticed she did this. Most Academy students read notation by naming intervals out loud, like spelling a word letter by letter. Lenne heard it.

They worked through the rest methodically. When Perin mentioned an interval — a tuning problem, an acoustic adjustment, a frequency that wasn't sitting right in a particular room — Lenne called it and Cass wrote it: the interval name, the direction, the modification if there was one. Quarter-flat. Minor-adjacent. Closer to a sixth than a fifth.

By the fifth hour they had a sequence.

Lenne looked at it. "Fourteen intervals across ten years."

"One per letter. Sometimes two. He was careful."

"He was slow on purpose." She touched the page with one finger. "One interval out of place and it reads as a tuning note."

"Not a message."

"He trusted you to hear the difference." She looked up. "How old were you when he sent the first one?"

"Four."

She was quiet for a moment. "He waited until you were old enough that someone would save the letters. He calculated that."

Cass had understood this already, sitting alone at his desk the night before, but hearing it said was different. It made the calculation real — Perin working it out, deciding: *Cass will be old enough when he's about four. I'll start then.* Ten years of patience. Ten years of writing careful letters that said *I hope you're well* and *tell Father the Ninth of Coppergate needs its clapper re-set* and also, underneath, in the gaps between the technical observations: *here is the thing I cannot say directly.*

His throat felt strange.

"What does it look like to you?" he said. "The sequence."

Lenne studied the paper. Her right index finger moved against her thigh, tracing intervals — a fourth, a fifth, back to the root. She was hearing it internally. After a moment she said, "It's not in any key. It's not avoiding a key either — it's not like chromatic passages where you're moving through keys. It's outside. Like it was composed before keys existed."

"I think it was."

She looked at him.

"The original bells," Cass said. "Aldric's bells. They predate the Codex. They predate the codified scales."

A silence. Lenne set her notation paper on the floor. "Your family's bells."

"My family's bells."

"The ones in the locked room."

He looked at her.

"You mentioned it last year," she said. "The corridor between the theory rooms. You said there were three bells in your house you weren't allowed to touch. You said it like it was nothing." Her finger stilled against her thigh. "It wasn't nothing."

He hadn't remembered saying it. He felt a brief flash of irritation at himself, and then something else — a relief he didn't quite want to name. He'd been carrying this alone for six months and she'd been holding a small piece of it for a year without knowing what it was.

"The bells in the locked room," he said. "I think they're tuned to this." He held up the paper. "To this phrase."

Lenne looked at it. "Have you tried? Singing it near the door?"

"Not yet."

She waited.

"I was working up to it," he said.

She nodded slowly, as if this made complete sense, and did not push. He was grateful for that. He picked up the lute from the wall peg and tuned it by ear — faster than the peg-and-mallet method, less precise. He got the lower strings close enough.

He played the phrase.

It wasn't right on the lute. The instrument wanted to pull the notes into the nearest recognized pitch, the way a cart wheel finds a rut. He had to press against that pull, hold the strings at slightly wrong positions. The sound that came out was close.

Lenne listened with her head turned a few degrees left. "There," she said, when he reached the third interval. "Stop. Play that one again."

He played it again.

"That's the one that makes my teeth feel wrong." She worked her jaw, then stopped. "What is that?"

"It doesn't have a name in the Codex."

She thought about it. "You know when you've been holding something heavy for a long time and you put it down, and your arms still feel the weight for a few seconds after? Like the weight is still there even though it isn't?"

"Yes."

"That. That interval feels like that."

He played the full phrase through. Lenne didn't speak. Her finger traced something against her thigh — not the phrase, something adjacent to it, a response. He watched her work through it.

"It's the second interval," she said when he stopped. "The almost-minor-sixth. A minor sixth is two notes pulling away from each other — unresolved. You know when a conversation ends wrong, when something's been said that needs answering and the person's already walked away? That." She tapped her own notation paper. "But this is flatter. This is a minor sixth that doesn't know it's a minor sixth yet. Still deciding."

Cass stared at her. "I've been trying to describe that for six months."

"My mother makes me do interval identification exercises. She plays something and I have to name what it makes me feel before I name what it is." Lenne's expression shifted into something not quite comfortable. "She says the Academy teaches it backwards. Name the interval, then describe the feeling. She says that's like learning the word for red before you've seen a color."

"She's right."

"Don't tell her I agreed. She'll assign more exercises." Lenne stood, picking up her coat from the floor. "We should go."

"We should go now?"

She looked at him. "You've had this for ten years. You've had the sequence for one night. You were going to sit on it for how much longer?"

He couldn't argue with that. He put the lute back on its peg, folded the paper into his coat pocket, and followed her down the stairs.

---

They went to the locked room first.

The kitchen was quiet. His father's boots were gone from the door, the workshop sounds faint through the wall — slow finishing work, the kind that needed patience. Cass stood in front of the door and Lenne stood a few feet back, giving him room he hadn't asked for but appreciated.

The phrase was still sitting just behind his teeth.

He sang it, quietly, in the kitchen.

The response came through the door.

His cheek was a foot from the wood and the sound reached him through the air first, just barely, then the vibration found the door itself and traveled through it — into his jaw, down into his chest, settling there in a way that was less like hearing and more like confirmation. Nothing had struck the bells. This was what bells did when a nearby pitch matched their own: they answered without being touched, the bronze waking up, each one a slightly different shade of the same phrase.

Three notes. Three resonances, lower than his voice, fuller. Bronze cast for this purpose and tuned to hold this frequency.

He stopped singing. The resonance faded slowly.

Behind him, Lenne said nothing. He turned and she had one hand flat against the wall, her expression careful and strange.

"You heard that," he said.

"I felt it. In my sternum." She took her hand off the wall. "Those bells have been in there the whole time."

"My whole life."

"Waiting for someone to give them the phrase." She looked at the door, then at him. "Cass. Your father knows this is in there."

"Yes."

"He's known your whole life."

"Yes."

A silence. Then Lenne picked up her bag from the chair. "We need to see Dav Sorn."

---

She wrote the letter of introduction at the Academy desk while Cass gathered the papers — the tally-songs from Torvald, the lame chord pattern, the co-custodial clause, the sealed record. The case he had been building in the language the system understood, because he had believed the system was the tool he needed. He still believed it. He needed to believe it a little longer.

But underneath the case, underneath the argument about intervals and ratification keys and contract form, there was the sequence on the folded paper in his coat pocket. Fourteen intervals. Ten years. The thing beneath the Civic Pitch.

The mid-ring smelled of rendered tallow and old paper. Dav Sorn's building had no sign — just a brass knocker shaped like a treble clef, worn smooth at the grip.

Lenne knocked. Cass stood half a step behind her and listened to the building settle.

A clerk took the letter of introduction, left them in a narrow hallway with a bench and a coat-hook and a single window that looked onto the alley wall. Lenne stood with her back against the plaster. Her right index finger traced intervals against her thigh — a fourth, a fifth, back to the root, then again. Cass sat on the bench. The stone was cold through his coat. He put his papers on his knees and looked at the window and said nothing.

The building had a resonance of its own — not a lie, nothing like that, just the quality of old limestone that had been absorbing sound for a hundred years. It pressed against the back of his jaw in a way that wasn't painful. More like pressure. Like the air before a storm.

He counted intervals under his breath. Not words. Just the numbers.

The clerk returned. *Follow me.*

---

Sorn was already reading Vella Ashvale's letter when they entered. He stood at his desk, two fingers resting against his throat just below the jaw, and he did not look up until he'd finished. Then he set the letter down with one flat motion and looked at Cass.

"Vella Ashvale does not write letters of introduction lightly."

"No," Lenne said. "She doesn't."

He gestured at the two chairs across the desk. The office was small and very orderly — not the orderliness of someone who didn't work, but of someone who needed to be able to find things quickly. Stacks of paper with weighted stones on top. A shelf of Codex volumes with slips of paper marking pages. On the desk, a tuning fork and a small bronze disc, the kind used for testing coin pitch.

Sorn sat. He was slight, with the upright posture of someone trained in breath control. He touched his throat again briefly — two fingers, just below the jaw — and then moved his hand to the desk.

"You've brought documentation," he said, looking at the papers on Cass's knees.

"Yes."

"Then start with the documentation. We can move to the other matter afterward." He picked up a pen. "The Bellwright contract with the House of Corda."

Cass laid out the papers in order. The original contract form, as he'd reconstructed it from the registrar's record. The tally-songs Torvald had given him, showing the pattern of cases where Corda-affiliated Court Singers had been unavailable. The co-custodial clause, flagged. The penalty terms.

Sorn read without speaking. His pen moved occasionally, marking something in the margin of his own notes. His face stayed neutral — not the performed neutrality of someone hiding a reaction, but the working neutrality of a man who had learned not to commit to a position before he'd finished reading.

When he reached the end, he set the papers down and looked at the tuning fork on his desk.

"The co-custodial clause," he said. "You believe it creates a conflict of interest in the original ratification."

"The ratification was witnessed by a Court Singer retained by the House of Corda," Cass said. "The contract binds a member of the family that holds co-custodial responsibility for the bells. The witnessing Singer had a financial relationship with the contracting party."

"That argument has been made before," Sorn said. "Twice, in my recollection. Both times the Academy ruled that the Court Singer's role was procedural — witnessing form, not adjudicating terms. The conflict-of-interest doctrine doesn't extend to procedural witnesses."

"I know. But those cases didn't have the lame chord."

Sorn's pen stopped.

"The ratification," Cass said. "The fifth resolves up, not down. I've checked the registrar's notation three times. An ascending fifth is a formal rejection. The contract was ratified with the wrong interval."

Sorn looked at him. Two fingers went to his throat.

"That would make it," he said, carefully, "not a contract."

"A gesture," Cass said. "Legally a declaration. Not a binding."

A silence. Lenne had gone very still in her chair.

Sorn picked up the papers again and went to the page with the ratification notation. He looked at it for a long time. His fingers moved against his desk in a small, contained way — not the bronze ring striking, not Maret Corda's gesture, but something similar. Working through a problem.

"Where is the standing-wave record?" he said.

Cass looked at him.

"The registrar keeps a standing-wave record for every ratified contract," Sorn said. "An acoustician measures the binding wave at the point of ratification and records its frequency and amplitude. If the fifth resolved upward, the wave would be — absent, or malformed. The record would show it." He set the papers down. "Where is it?"

"I couldn't find it," Cass said. "I requested it from the registrar's office three days ago. They said the record was in the archive. When I went to the archive, the clerk said it had been transferred to the Academy for review. When I went to the Academy, they said they had no record of receiving it."

Sorn looked at the tuning fork.

"The standing-wave record for the Bellwright contract ratification is missing," he said.

"Yes."

"That is —" He stopped. Started again. "In my experience, records of that kind do not go missing through administrative error. They are not the kind of document that gets misfiled." He picked up his pen and set it down again. "How long ago was the ratification?"

"Ten years."

"Ten years." He touched his throat. "And the record has been missing for —"

"I don't know. I'm the first person who's tried to find it."

Another silence. Sorn stood and went to the window. It looked onto the same alley as the hallway window — a narrow strip of gray sky between two limestone walls. He stood with his hands clasped behind his back.

"I have sung forty-three verdicts in the last two years," he said. He was looking at the alley. "I have been satisfied with eleven of them."

Cass said nothing. Lenne's finger had stopped moving against her thigh.

"The remaining thirty-two," Sorn said, "were — not wrong, exactly. The form was correct. The intervals were correct. But the outcomes —" He stopped. "The outcomes reflected the resources of the parties more than the merits of the case. In each instance, the merits were genuine. The form was genuine. The outcome was still predictable from the start." He turned from the window. "I am telling you this because you should understand what you're asking me to do."

"What are we asking you to do?" Lenne said.

He looked at her. "You're asking me to file a formal challenge to a contract ratification on the grounds of a procedural defect that the Academy has already ruled irrelevant in analogous cases, using a standing-wave record that no longer exists, in a case where the contracting party is the House of Corda." He sat down. "The House of Corda retains two of the four Court Singers in this city. The other two are myself and your mother. If I file this challenge, the Academy will need to convene a review panel. The panel will require a Court Singer to sing the standing-wave reconstruction — to recreate, from existing records, what the binding wave would have looked like if the ratification was valid." He touched his throat again. "That Singer will be either me or your mother. And the House of Corda will know it."

"Will you do it?" Cass said.

Sorn looked at him. Something shifted in his expression — not softening, exactly. More like a man recognizing a question he had been waiting for.

"The sub-harmonic," he said.

Cass went still.

"Vella's letter mentioned a frequency beneath the Civic Pitch," Sorn said. "She described it as — and I'm quoting — *an interval outside the codified scales that her son has been hearing for some time, and which she believes may be relevant to the Bellwright case.* She was characteristically uninformative about why." He looked at Cass. "She wasn't writing about you, was she. She was writing about herself."

"She's been hearing it too," Cass said. "For years."

"As have I," Sorn said.

The room was very quiet.

"I've heard it since my third year of practice," he said. "The morning Civic Pitch, at the end of the sequence. Below the bells. I reported it once, to a senior Court Singer, when I was newly licensed. She told me it was acoustic fatigue — a perceptual artifact from too many hours in resonance chambers. I accepted this explanation because it was convenient." He looked at the tuning fork on his desk. "I have not accepted it since."

"What do you think it is?" Lenne said.

"I think it is a frequency that Aldric Bellwright put into the bells two hundred years ago, that the Codex has no language for, and that the Academy would prefer not to have to explain." He picked up the tuning fork. Set it down. "I think it is why the Bellwright family journals are sealed. And I think it is connected to why a standing-wave record from ten years ago no longer exists in any archive in this city."

Cass reached into his coat pocket and took out the folded paper. He unfolded it and set it on the desk between them.

Sorn looked at it.

"These are from my brother's letters," Cass said. "One interval per letter, over ten years. He's been encoding a phrase."

Sorn read the sequence. His expression didn't change. But his breathing changed — a small, controlled adjustment, the kind a trained singer made when something hit them and they didn't want it to show.

"This is the sub-harmonic," he said.

"Yes."

"Your brother has been sending you the sub-harmonic. Piece by piece. From inside the House of Corda." Sorn set the paper down. He looked at the wall for a moment. Then: "He knows what it is."

"I think so. I think he found out when he was sixteen and it's why Maret Corda wanted him."

"Not just a good composer," Sorn said. "Someone who already knew what was in the bells." He touched his throat. "Someone who couldn't be allowed to tell anyone else."

The afternoon light was moving across the desk. Somewhere in the alley, a cart went past, the wheel-noise muffled by the limestone.

"The case I can make," Sorn said, "is the lame chord. The ascending fifth. Even without the standing-wave record, I can argue that the record's absence is itself evidence of irregularity — a missing record in a case involving the House of Corda, where the standing-wave record for every other contract from that period exists intact. That is not nothing. It is not sufficient on its own, but it is not nothing." He straightened the papers on his desk. "The sub-harmonic is a different matter. I cannot bring that before the Academy. I cannot walk into a review panel and say *there is a frequency in the Civic Pitch that the Codex doesn't recognize, and a sixteen-year-old boy was bound into service because he knew about it.* They will rule it inadmissible. They will question my fitness to practice."

"But you believe it," Cass said.

"What I believe is not the point." Sorn's voice was careful. Precise. "What I can argue before a panel is the point. And what I can argue before a panel is the lame chord, the missing record, and the conflict-of-interest doctrine, which — as I said — has been ruled irrelevant in analogous cases. That is a difficult case. It is not an impossible one."

"Will you take it?"

Sorn was quiet for a long moment. He picked up the tuning fork and held it, not striking it. Just held it.

"I need to think," he said. "There are — implications I need to work through. If I file this challenge, I am filing it against the House of Corda, in a city where the House of Corda retains half the Court Singers. The consequences for my practice if I lose are significant." He set the tuning fork down. "I'm not saying no. I'm saying come back tomorrow."

"Tomorrow," Cass said.

"I know that's not what you wanted to hear."

It wasn't. But he also heard what was underneath it — a man who had been satisfied with eleven verdicts out of forty-three, sitting with a tuning fork in his hand, not saying no. That was different from no. That was a man deciding something.

"Tomorrow," Cass said.

Sorn nodded. He picked up the papers — the tally-songs, the contract form, the co-custodial clause — and squared them into a neat stack. He did not hand them back.

"Leave the documentation," he said. "I'll want to read it again."

Cass left the folded paper too, the sequence of fourteen intervals, the phrase his brother had been building for ten years. He almost picked it up again at the door. He didn't.

---

Outside, the mid-ring afternoon was cold and bright. Lenne walked beside him and neither of them spoke for a block.

"Eleven out of forty-three," she said.

"I know."

"That's a man who's been keeping count." She pulled her coat tighter. "He's already decided. He just hasn't said it yet."

Cass thought about the tuning fork in Sorn's hand. The careful, qualified language of a person who had learned what words cost and was measuring out exactly how much he was willing to spend.

"Maybe," he said.

"The sub-harmonic," Lenne said. "He's heard it for years and never told anyone."

"Neither had your mother."

She was quiet for a moment. "She's going to ask me how it went."

"What will you tell her?"

"The truth." Lenne glanced at him. "That there's a Court Singer who's heard the same thing she has, and he thinks it matters, and we're going back tomorrow." She paused. "She's going to want to come."

Cass thought about that. Vella Ashvale, who had been hearing the sub-harmonic for years and had kept it from the Academy, who had written a letter of introduction that was characteristically uninformative about why. A Court Singer who already knew there was a frequency the Codex couldn't name.

"Let her," he said.

They turned into the Bell Quarter. The workshop sounds reached him through the wall — still the slow finishing work, steady. His father at the lathe, hands quiet, giving them something to do.

The locked room was still in there. The three bells still tuned to the phrase. And somewhere in the House of Corda's compound, Perin was at a composing table, doing work that was genuinely good, in a room that was comfortable enough, writing careful letters with technical observations that weren't only technical observations.

*A minor sixth that doesn't know it's a minor sixth yet. Still deciding.*

Tomorrow Sorn would either say yes or he wouldn't. The case would move or it wouldn't. But the sequence was on the paper on Sorn's desk now, out of Cass's coat pocket, out of the box under the floorboard, no longer only his. Fourteen intervals. Ten years. His brother's handwriting in forty-three letters, composing a question piece by piece and trusting that someone would eventually hear the answer in it.

Cass went inside. The kitchen was quiet. He stood in front of the locked door for a moment, not singing, just standing there.

Then he went upstairs to wait for tomorrow.
---

# Chapter Nine: The Acoustician's Report

Cass had slept badly. The Sorn meeting sat in his chest — the shared frequency, the cautious hope — but when he came downstairs at dawn, his father was already in the workshop with a stranger.

The acoustician had arrived at seven, early enough that the morning Civic Pitch still hung in the air above the Bell Quarter, its last harmonics bleeding out of the limestone. His name was Ferren, around forty, with the careful posture of a man trained in breath work. He carried his reference instrument in a leather case strapped across his back and introduced himself with a card rather than a handshake — heavy stock, copper-inked, the Corda house mark in the corner.

Eddan took the card without reading it. "The tower's open. I'll show you the access sequence."

He did not introduce Cass. Cass followed anyway.

There was a woman already at the tower door.

Cass nearly walked into her. She was small and upright, dressed in dark navy, standing with her hands at her sides in the stillness of someone who had been waiting long enough to stop looking like they were waiting. Gray hair, close-cropped. A bronze ring on her left hand. She was looking at the Bell Tower's upper fittings, where the morning light caught the bronze bright, and she did not turn when they came up the path.

Then she did.

"Eddan," she said. One word. A statement, not a greeting.

Eddan stopped. "Maret."

Cass knew the name before his father said it. He had known it for ten years, the way you knew the name of a thing you'd been circling without being allowed to look at directly. He made himself stay still. He made himself breathe.

Maret Corda looked at him — not the way adults looked at children, deciding how much to explain. The way she'd looked at the tower fittings: assessing something that fell within her field of concern.

"The co-custodial clause grants oversight," she said. To Eddan, not to Cass. "I have the right to be present."

"I know the clause," Eddan said.

She nodded once and turned back to the tower door. The right existed. She was here. These were the same fact.

No under-note.

He had expected something — the greasy quarter-tone, the needle behind his eye. She was the reason Perin was gone. She was the reason his father's hands shook. She was standing six feet from him in the morning light and the air between them was acoustically clean, and that was somehow worse than anything he'd prepared for.

The four of them went up the stairs.

---

Ferren set his case on the floor in the center of the bell chamber and opened it with the care of a man who had done this many hundreds of times and had not stopped being careful. Inside, nested in fitted leather: a reference mallet, a resonance meter in a brass housing, a ledger already open to a fresh page.

"I'll work sequentially," he said. "Registered pitch first, then resonance decay. I'll note any deviation above a quarter-tone." He picked up the mallet. "The decay curve tells me more than the strike. A repaired bell will often ring true on impact and then drift. That's what I'm listening for."

He struck the first bell.

The sound filled the chamber completely, immediately — a high, clear D-sharp that rang off the limestone vault and came back enriched, the other bells answering in sympathy. Cass felt it in his sternum before he heard it with his ears. Ferren watched the needle. He wrote. He made no other movement for nearly a minute.

He moved to the second bell.

The morning light came through the east window in a rectangle across the floor, pale gold. The second bell was the Ash Street sequence bell, a B-natural, and when Ferren struck it the sound had a quality Cass had never managed to describe adequately — something in the overtone series sitting exactly right against the limestone.

"Good sustain on this one," Ferren said, not quite to himself. "The chamber geometry helps. You get a natural extension on the B-natural because the east wall's at the right angle." He moved on.

"What's he writing?" Cass said, low, to his father.

Eddan didn't look at him. "Pitch and condition."

"Both columns?"

"Don't."

Cass looked at his father's profile. The set of his jaw. He looked at the floor.

Maret stood on the far side of the chamber, near the east wall. She had not moved since they'd come up the stairs. She watched Ferren's meter needle with the attention she would have given a Court Singer mid-ratification, and she did not look at Eddan, and Eddan did not look at her.

Eddan's hands were at his sides. In the workshop, they had a life of their own — moving to the next tool before the last motion had finished, the tremor settling into purposeful work. Here they were simply still. A different stillness. Something held.

Cass knew which bells were coming. He had grown up with them the way you grew up with the layout of your own house — not by memorizing it but by living in it until the knowledge was in your feet. He knew the sequence, the positions, the register of each bell's voice. He knew which six had been repaired. The first repaired bell was the twenty-fourth.

Ferren was at the eleventh. Cass did the arithmetic without wanting to.

The bells came in their sequence. The silence between the four of them had its own texture by now — not the comfortable silence of people with nothing to say, but something denser, a silence that knew what it was waiting for.

"Does the decay curve on the repaired bells look different," Cass said.

Eddan's head turned slightly. A warning in the angle of it.

Ferren glanced at him. "Depends on the repair. A good repair on a cracked bell — if the craftsman knows what he's doing — the decay profile can be nearly identical to an unrepaired bell. The difference is usually in the overtone series, not the fundamental." He looked at the meter. "You have to know what you're listening for."

"What are you listening for?"

"Drift," Ferren said. "A repaired bell sometimes shows a slight slope in the decay — the repaired section vibrates at a fractionally different rate than the original material. It's small. You'd miss it in a busy market." He tapped the meter housing. "You don't miss it in here."

Cass looked at his father.

Eddan was looking at the bell.

The twenty-fourth hung between two originals, and you would not know it by looking. Three years of Eddan's work had made the repair invisible: the new metal seated into the old casting, the clapper rebalanced, the pitch-collar adjusted by fractions until the registered frequency was exact. Cass had watched him do it. He had stood in the workshop on a winter afternoon and watched his father's hands — steady, absolutely steady, the tremor gone — work the alloy into the bell's damaged shoulder with a patience that had nothing of grief in it. Only craft.

Ferren raised the mallet.

He struck.

The bell rang true. Completely, cleanly true — the registered D-flat blooming into the chamber air with no hesitation, no thinness. The meter needle swung to the correct position and held there, steady as a plumb line, and Cass's jaw was tight.

Ferren stood with his head tilted, watching the needle. "Clean strike. Good pitch." He watched the decay. "The sustain is —" He paused. Wrote something. "Slightly shorter than the adjacent originals. Not significant on its own." He wrote again. "I'll note it."

"What does that mean for the report," Cass said.

"Cass." Eddan's voice was flat.

"I'm asking a technical question."

Ferren looked at him with the equanimity of a man who had been asked technical questions by anxious families many times before. "It means the repair is documented. The bell rings to its registered pitch. The repair is noted." A pause. "The report reflects what is, not what was."

He wrote the asterisk. Cass could see the motion of the pen, the two small strokes. *Repair confirmed, original alloy components unverified.*

The bell rang true. His father had made it ring true. Three years ago, with hands that shook at the dinner table and settled at the lathe, Eddan Bellwright had repaired this bell until it was indistinguishable from the original by any measurement any instrument could take.

The bell rang true. That was not the point.

Cass pressed his Maret's eyes moved to Eddan's face. Eddan was looking at the bell.

Then Cass was watching through the gap between two iron frames, Maret thinking herself unobserved, and her hand went to the twenty-fourth bell. Not the way Ferren touched it, clinical, testing. The way Eddan touched a bell when he was listening for its history. Her fingertips found the join where the old alloy met the new, the seam Eddan had made invisible, and she traced it. Slowly. The way you'd trace a scar on someone you used to know.

Her lips moved. No sound came out, nothing Cass could hear, nothing the under-note could register. But her mouth shaped something, and from the angle he was watching it looked like a note. A single interval. Something learned from a father who tuned by ear.

Then her hand dropped. Her face settled back. She clasped her hands behind her back and turned to watch Ferren, and the moment was over.

Cass stood among the bells and thought: *she can hear them too.*

Not the way he could. Not the under-note, not the sub-harmonic. But the bronze. The alloy. The difference between a bell that was whole and a bell that had been broken and mended. She had known the six repaired bells before Ferren tested a single one. She had known because she had touched them, and her hands knew bronze the way her father's hands had known it, and the Academy had told her that knowledge didn't count.

He looked at her and looked at his father's back and held that for a moment — the two of them, Eddan and Maret, both with hands that knew bronze, both in this room, both watching a man with a meter do what their hands could have done in thirty seconds. What the system required instead of what would have been true.

Ferren worked through the remaining bells. Each repaired one rang true. Each received the asterisk. At the fortieth bell — an original, ringing with the warmth of two-hundred-year-old bronze — he paused.

"The repair work is exceptional," he said. "Whoever did it understood the alloy." He glanced at Eddan. "The decay slope is minimal. Most repair work I see, the drift is obvious within the first two seconds. This held for nearly four."

Eddan said, "Thank you."

Flat. Ferren nodded and moved on.

The sixth repaired bell was the forty-first. When Ferren struck it, Cass was watching his father's hands. At his sides. Still. The stillness of a man who had decided this was the place where his hands didn't shake, and he was going to hold to that, and it was costing him something.

The forty-first bell rang true.

Ferren made the asterisk.

He closed the ledger. He capped the meter. He fitted the mallet back into its case and pressed the catches down with both thumbs, and the soft click of them was the only sound in the chamber.

"Six bells have been repaired rather than recast," he said. "The repairs are technically sound. The bells ring true to their registered pitch." He paused. "The custodianship agreement specifies maintenance of the original forty-seven bells. A repaired bell is not the same instrument as the original."

He said it without apology and without particular emphasis. A measurement.

"I'll have the report to the House of Corda by this afternoon."

Eddan said, "I see."

Maret said nothing. She moved to the stairwell and went down without waiting for Ferren. Her footsteps were unhurried on the left-winding stairs. They faded.

Ferren nodded once and followed.

The tower was quiet. Forty-seven bells hung in their sequence, and the light through the high vents had gone flat and white with midday, and Cass's father stood in the middle of it with his hands at his sides.

"What does fitness mean," Cass said.

Eddan turned toward the stairwell.

"Dad." Cass took a step after him. "What does fitness give her."

Eddan stopped. He didn't turn around. His hands — Cass could see them from here — had started shaking. The fine tremor. The one that didn't show at the lathe.

"The right to determine whether the current arrangement is —" Eddan stopped. Tried again. "Whether we're —" He didn't finish that either. "That's what fitness means."

"And if she determines it isn't."

"Co-custodial oversight. Section Twelve."

"Her people in the tower. Every morning. For the Civic Pitch." Cass heard his own voice going flat, the way it went when he was trying to hold something very still. "So she'd have people with their hands on the bells that set the reference pitch for every contract ratified in the city. Every day. Until the old-alloy stock is sufficient to recast six bells." He stopped. "Which it isn't. And hasn't been."

Eddan turned then. His face was composed, his hands were not, and he looked at Cass with the specific exhaustion of a man who had known this conversation was coming and had spent ten years hoping he'd die before it arrived.

"How long has the stock been too low," Cass said.

"Seven years."

"She's known for seven years that she had this."

"Maret has known the stock was low since before I did."

The quiet in the chamber was complete. Forty-seven bells, the light flat, the wind through the arched windows with its breath-across-a-jar sound.

"And Perin's been there for ten." His voice went somewhere he hadn't meant it to go. "So you've spent seven of those ten years knowing she had this and — "

"Don't." Eddan's voice cracked on the word. One syllable. It landed in the room like a struck bell, resonating in the silence, and neither of them moved.

Cass looked at his father's shaking hands. He looked at his father's face. *He knows. He has always known. He has been knowing it every morning at the lathe and every evening at the dinner table and every night alone with Perin's letters, and knowing it has not moved him.*

"I'm going to Torvald," Cass said.

Eddan closed his eyes.

"He knows about the co-custodial clauses. The Compact has records — "

"I know what Torvald Hess knows."

"Then you know he might be able to — "

"Cass." His father opened his eyes. Something in his face that was not quite a plea and not quite a warning. The face of a man asking his son to stop before they both went somewhere they couldn't come back from. "Not today."

Cass looked at him for a long moment.

"The report comes this afternoon," he said.

"I know."

"So when."

Eddan turned back to the stairwell. He started down. His footsteps were careful on the worn stone, one hand on the wall.

Cass stood at the top of the stairs and listened to his father descend and did not follow.

---

The report arrived at half-past three. Eddan brought it inside, set it on the kitchen table without opening it, stood there a moment, then went to the workshop.

Cass picked it up.

Four pages, Ferren's neat hand on heavy Corda stock. The first two pages were technical — each bell by number, pitch, condition. The six repaired bells marked with asterisks: *repair confirmed, original alloy components unverified.* The third page was the analysis. The fourth was the conclusion.

He read the conclusion twice.

*Given the above findings, the House of Corda respectfully notes that the current state of six bells in the forty-seven-bell civic sequence constitutes a discrepancy between the present condition of the instrument set and the terms of the Bellwright-Corda custodianship agreement, which specifies the maintenance of the original forty-seven bells in their original composition. This discrepancy may be resolved by one of two means: first, by recasting the six affected bells from original-alloy bronze to restore compliance; or second, by the invocation of co-custodial oversight under Section Twelve of the custodianship agreement, to remain in effect until the discrepancy is resolved to the satisfaction of both parties. The House of Corda defers to the Bellwright family as to the preferred resolution, while noting that the fitness of the current arrangement to fulfill the terms of the agreement is a matter that warrants prompt attention.*

*Fitness.* Sitting in the middle of that last sentence like a stone dropped in still water.

He set the report down. He sat with it in front of him and the kitchen going dim around him. Seven years. She had known the stock was insufficient for seven years and she had waited, and the waiting was its own kind of craft — the patience of someone who understood that the system moved slowly and had planned accordingly. She had stood in the tower this morning and watched a man with a meter confirm what her hands had already told her, and her face had given nothing, and she had gone down the stairs without waiting.

He thought about Perin's ratification line. The small handwriting. The quarter-tone flat.

He thought about eleven satisfactions out of forty-three.

He stood up. He walked to the door of the locked room.

He had passed this door every day of his life. Solid oak, old enough that the grain had gone dark with years of hand-oil at the latch, the lock a newer addition — the new metal slightly brighter than the surrounding wood. He pressed his ear to it.

The three bells inside were humming.

Not the way the workshop bells hummed in sympathy with the Civic Pitch. This was something else. A low vibration he felt in his cheekbone and jaw before he heard it with his ear. Sub-harmonic. Below the range of the morning Pitch, below the range of anything he could name.

It pressed against him like a question.

He stood with his ear against the door and the question pressed against his jaw and the word *fitness* sat in the report on the kitchen table behind him, and the lathe ran in the workshop, and his father worked in the only place his guilt couldn't reach him, and somewhere across the city in the Merchant Tier compound Maret Corda had a copy of the same four pages.

He thought about her fingertips on the seam of the twenty-fourth bell. Her mouth shaping a note that made no sound.

The humming did not change. It had no urgency. It had no answer. It was just there, the way it had always been there, waiting for him to have the vocabulary.

He lifted his ear from the door.

He went to find Torvald.
---

# Chapter Ten: What Torvald Knows

Torvald listened the way the city's bowl caught sound — without effort, without interrupting, letting everything settle to the floor.

Cass talked for a long time. He laid out the inspection report, the language about *fitness*, Section Twelve and what it triggered, the old-alloy stock his father knew was insufficient and had known for years. Then the co-custodial clause, which he had to explain twice because his voice went flat the first time and he wasn't sure he'd said it in the right order. Then the lame chord — the way Perin's ratification note had sat a quarter-tone low, the specific texture of it, greasy and unresolved, the kind of thing you'd hear if you were listening for it and miss entirely if you weren't.

Torvald's expression didn't change. He cupped his left ear slightly and kept his eyes on Cass.

The sub-harmonic in the bells took the longest to explain. Cass had not said it aloud to anyone before — not the full version, not the part about how it felt like a question rather than a sound, how it was lowest on mornings when his father's hands shook worst at breakfast and then settled by the time they reached the workshop. Saying it to Torvald's face, in the Compact's back room with its smell of old dye and pine resin, felt like striking a test note against a practice bell he'd never tuned. He didn't know what the resonance would be.

He stopped talking.

Torvald was quiet. On the Dyer's Road, a cart horse shifted its weight and one of the wheel-spokes knocked against the cobble. Past the Fade Line, that sound was just a sound.

"The inspection was today," Cass said, because the silence had started to feel like something he'd broken.

"Yes," Torvald said.

"And."

"I heard you."

He stood up, crossed to the window, looked out at nothing in particular. His hands, blue-green from thirty years of dye work, hung at his sides. He turned back after a moment.

"You're describing a system that — look, it's like a dye that doesn't stain. You dip the cloth and it comes out changed but there's nothing on your hands, nothing in the vat, no trace of the color anywhere except in the fabric itself. Coercion without fingerprints." He said it the way Cass said *that fifth is flat* — as an observation, a thing the ear simply caught. "I've been saying that for twenty years, in one way or another, to anyone who'd sit still long enough. Nobody listened because I don't have credentials and because a retired dye-merchant talking about contract law sounds about as serious as a bell-maker giving advice on indigo."

The under-note had been with him since the inspection; it hadn't gone anywhere.

"The report uses the word *fitness*," Cass said.

"Section Four of the custodianship agreement. Yes. She's had this ready for a while." Torvald came back to the table and sat down heavily. "The question is what you do with it. Not what she's done. What *you* do."

"I've been trying to find a mechanism inside the Codex. Something that would void the clause, or contest the inspection findings —"

"I know what you've been trying." Torvald's voice was not unkind. "The system just moved against you. That's the part you have to sit with for a minute."

Cass sat with it for approximately three seconds. "So what else is there."

Torvald almost smiled. He reached under the table and produced a flat wooden box, the kind used for keeping tally-songs — notched records, strips of scored wood that the Academy didn't recognize and the Compact relied on completely. He didn't open it. He set it in front of him and pressed both palms on the lid.

"There's a group," he said. "Informal. No name they use publicly. I call them the Blank Pages Society because that's what they care about — the back of Volume Four."

Cass had heard of the blank pages. Every Academy student had. Forty-three of them, sitting at the end of the volume that was supposed to cover exceptional circumstances and contested frequencies. The official explanation was that they were reserved for future additions. The official explanation had been the official explanation for a hundred and sixty years.

"Scholars," Torvald said. "A few frustrated Court Singers. People who think the blank pages are deliberate — that someone left them empty on purpose and the reason for it matters. I know two of them personally." He paused. "One is a retired lecturer. Taught acoustical theory at the Academy for thirty years before they put her out. Her name is Mira Fen."

Cass heard the name and something in his chest did a strange, short movement. "Proctor Fen's mother."

"Yes."

He almost laughed. He stopped himself, but the shape of it was there — the specific, wrong-key humor of finding out that the proctor who had docked his marks on a contract theory exercise last spring was the son of the woman who thought the whole system had a gap in it. , but it gave the laugh somewhere to go.

"Who's the other one," he said.

Torvald looked at him steadily. "Dav Sorn."

The name landed wrong. Cass turned it over, checked the pitch of it, checked again. "The Court Singer."

"He's been attending meetings for three years. Hasn't acted on anything they've discussed." Torvald spread his hands briefly — not a shrug, just an acknowledgment of the shape of the thing. "He's there. That's not nothing."

Dav Sorn's voice in a courtroom — the precise, hedged sentences, the way he touched his throat when he was working through something. *In my assessment. Based on available evidence.* A man who had composed forty-three verdicts and been satisfied with eleven of them. Sitting in a room with scholars who believed the system had a deliberate hole in it. For three years. Doing nothing.

He didn't say anything. The thought sat in his chest.

"The Corda woman," Torvald said. "She knows something about the bells that she's been using as pressure. Not the inspection — that's new, that's the mechanism she chose to use the pressure with. The pressure itself is older. She's had it for thirty years."

"The journals," Cass said.

"Whatever's in them. Yes."

"They're in the locked room."

"Yes."

Cass looked at the tally-box under Torvald's hands. "My father has the key."

"Yes." Torvald's voice was level. He let the silence run for a moment. "I'm not telling you to steal from your father, boy. I'm telling you the answer is in that room."

The difference between those two statements was very small. Cass could hear it — the gap between them, a quarter-tone at most, the kind of interval that looked clean on paper and sat wrong in the air.

He nodded once.

The door opened.

Lenne came in carrying a fold of paper in one hand and her coat in the other, the coat still half-on because she'd been moving quickly and hadn't stopped to sort herself out. She looked at Torvald, then at Cass, then at Torvald again.

"You started without me," she said.

"You were late."

"I was gathering information." She dropped into the chair across from Cass and got her second arm into the coat sleeve with the focused efficiency of someone who has already moved on from the coat problem. She put the paper on the table. "My mother was approached."

Cass went still.

"The House of Corda," Lenne said. "They sent a representative to the Ashvale house four days ago. Asked her to ratify the co-custodial transfer — the bells moving from Bellwright custody to joint Corda-Bellwright management." She said it the way she said everything: precisely, with the Academy's careful diction, but underneath it something that wasn't quite level. "She refused."

"Good," Cass said.

"She's now under pressure." Lenne's finger moved on the table — a small, unconscious gesture, fingering an interval she wasn't playing. "The Corda-retained Court Singers filed a complaint with the Academy two days ago. About a verdict my mother sang in the Merchant Quarter last spring. They're claiming the interval resolution was irregular."

The spring verdict. Cass remembered Lenne saying something about it months ago — *I watched. The form was perfect. I've been thinking about it since.* He looked at her face. Her jaw was set.

"Was it?" he said.

"No." Short. Certain. "The form was correct. I was there. I watched her tune the ratification note against the reference bell before she sang a single phrase." She picked up the paper and put it down again. "The complaint is — it's a message. That's all it is."

"Does your mother know you're here?" Torvald said.

A pause. "She told me to stay out of it."

"And."

Lenne looked at him. "I'm here."

Torvald made a sound that was not quite approval and not quite anything else. He leaned back in his chair and the old wood creaked under him. "The pressure is spreading," he said, to both of them and neither of them specifically. "That's what this is. The Corda woman has the inspection mechanism ready, she's moving on the bells, and anyone who might complicate that process is getting a message. The Ashvale complaint is the message."

"What do we do about it," Cass said.

"Nothing yet. Vella Ashvale is the best Court Singer in this city and she knows how to handle a complaint. That's not your problem right now." He looked at Cass. "Your problem is the locked room."

Lenne looked at Cass.

He didn't explain. She didn't ask. That was one of the things about Lenne — she gathered information and waited to see if it was offered, and if it wasn't, she filed it and moved on. He'd noticed it early and found it useful in a way he hadn't expected.

They stayed another half-hour. Torvald talked through what Mira Fen had published — three papers, none of them through the Academy, all of them circulated in the Compact's network — about the blank pages. The argument, roughly, was that Volume Four's exceptional-circumstances section had been left incomplete because completing it would require acknowledging that the system couldn't account for coercion. That the forty-three blank pages were a gap that someone had decided was better left unfilled than filled honestly.

"She can't prove it," Torvald said. "She has the structural argument. She needs the source material."

"The journals," Cass said.

"The journals."

The under-note had settled to a dull, persistent weight by the time they left. Outside, the Dyer's Road had gone to evening light — amber off the limestone, long shadows from the vat-buildings, the smell of indigo and something chemical underneath it that caught at the back of the throat. Lenne walked with him as far as the Fade Line.

"Mira Fen," she said, when they were nearly there.

"Yes."

"She's been to our house. My mother knows her." She paused. "I didn't know about the Society."

"Torvald said your mother knows things."

Lenne was quiet for a moment. "She knows more than she says. That's not — I mean, I've known that for a while. She watches my father's verdicts and doesn't say anything." She stopped walking. They were at the Fade Line now, the invisible boundary where the city's careful acoustics began to take hold. On this side, the cart horse and the dye-vat sounds and the normal noise of a district that talked out loud. On the other side, the lower, considered murmur of the inner city, where you kept your voice down because voices carried. "I've been thinking about the spring verdict," she said. "The Merchant Quarter one."

"You said the form was perfect."

"It was. That's the part I can't stop thinking about." She looked at him — not asking him to solve it, just saying it to someone who would hear it. "The form was correct and the outcome was wrong. I watched the whole thing. I know the intervals. And the outcome was wrong."

He didn't say anything. She wasn't asking him to.

"Good luck," she said. Not what she meant, and they both knew it, and she turned back toward the Weavers' District while he crossed the Fade Line and walked home through a city that had gone quiet for the evening.

---

The house was lit when he got there. His father's shadow moved behind the workshop's high window — still working, the Civic Pitch long done, the bells for the day all struck and settled. Cass stood in the narrow passage between the workshop wall and the kitchen door and looked up at the shadow for a moment. Then he went inside.

He ate the bread and cold soup his mother had left covered on the kitchen table. The kitchen was quiet. The wall to the locked room was two feet from where he sat, plain plaster, a door in it that had been locked since he was four years old and that he had never once tried to open.

He had always told himself there was a reason he'd never tried. That his father would tell him when he was ready, when the time was right, when Cass had learned enough to understand what was in there.

He was fourteen years old.

His brother had been in service for ten years, composing contract music for the House of Corda in a resonance chamber with acoustics better than the Academy practice rooms, writing letters about the weather and the quality of the bells and what he'd had for lunch. His father's hands shook at the breakfast table and settled at the lathe and had been doing both for ten years. The bells were going to be taken, or shared, or transferred, or whatever word the co-custodial clause used to describe the thing that was happening. The Corda woman had a document and a mechanism and thirty years of pressure built from something she'd read in journals that sat twenty feet from where Cass was eating cold soup.

His father had not told him anything.

He was ready.

He set the bowl down. The sound of it on the table was ordinary and specific — ceramic on wood, a small resonance that died in less than a second. He pressed his palm flat on the table beside the bowl and felt the grain of the wood under his hand, the slight roughness of it, the temperature.

The bells in the locked room were old — three of them, small, hung from iron brackets, cast from the same alloy as the forty-seven originals. He had never heard them ring. He had stood close to the door sometimes, in the kitchen, and heard nothing from the other side, which meant either the room was acoustically sealed or the bells were silent. He had always assumed silent.

He was no longer sure he'd assumed correctly.

There was a pitch the body knew before the ear did. He had felt it his whole life, in the mornings when the Civic Pitch sounded, in the specific harmonic that sat beneath the sequence like a current under still water — not painful, not quite pleasure, something older than either.

He was still afraid. He was going to open the room anyway.

His father kept spares of everything. Organized by type, on a board by the lathe — workshop keys on the left side, house keys on the right, each one on a labeled hook in Eddan's careful hand. Cass had grown up watching him hang them there after use, take them down before work, replace them exactly. It was the most orderly thing about him. The rest of the workshop was controlled chaos, bronze filings in the floor grit, bells in progress crowding the iron frames, half-finished clappers on every horizontal surface. But the key board was precise.

He had seen the locked room's key on that board. He had seen it for years, a small brass thing on a hook labeled *K. rm.* in his father's shorthand, and he had never taken it down because he had told himself there was a reason he was waiting.

He was done waiting.

He got up from the table. He rinsed the bowl at the basin and set it to dry. He went to bed, because his father was still in the workshop and there was no point going for the key with his father twenty feet away and the night still early. He lay in the dark and listened to the workshop sounds — the low rasp of the lathe, a test strike against a practice bell, the clean note of it ringing through the wall and then fading.

He didn't sleep for a long time.

Sometime past midnight, when the workshop had been silent for hours, he heard movement below. Not the workshop — the kitchen. The particular creak of the floorboard near the basin, then the heavier one by the locked room door. His father's tread, unmistakable even through the ceiling, the weight of him shifting from one foot to the other.

Then the sound of a key in a lock.

Cass held still. The door opened — he couldn't hear it open, but he heard the change in the house's acoustics, the way the air moved differently when that room was unsealed. Then nothing for a long time. Long enough that Cass thought his father might have gone in and closed the door behind him.

But then: a page turning. Not one page — several, in quick succession, the sound of someone flipping through something they'd read before, looking for a specific passage. The rustle stopped. A pause. Then his father's breathing, audible through the ceiling in the dead-quiet house, the long uneven exhale of a man reading something he already knew by heart and reading it again anyway.

It went on for ten minutes. Maybe fifteen. Then the pages again, slower this time. The door. The lock. The creak of the kitchen floorboard, and then the stairs, and then his father passing his door without stopping, the tremor in his footsteps that the lathe couldn't steady.
